The Karrami Objection: Consistency of Proof
The Karrami argues that the proof against direction would also disprove direction if established by proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Karramiyya objected to the proof against direction by claiming that the same reasoning could also prove direction.

Core Idea
A proof against something can be reversed to prove it.

The Karrami objection challenges the consistency of the Maturidi proof. It argues that if affirming direction implies temporal attributes, then denying direction implies non-existence, which is equally unacceptable. This forces a reconsideration of the proof's validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Karrami objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the structure of the argument.
Notice the parallel structure: 'what you mentioned of proofs necessitates...' and 'what you mentioned of proof necessitates...'.
2
Identify the two claims
Identify the two key claims: (1) affirming direction implies signs of temporality, (2) denying direction implies non-existence. Both are deemed unacceptable.
The Karrami is not rejecting proof but pointing out an inconsistency.
3
Understand the logical trap
Reflect on how the Karrami uses the author's own standard: just as proof cannot make a temporal thing eternal, it cannot negate something proven to exist.
This challenges the author to show why his proof against direction is stronger than any proof for direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللكرامي أن يقول : إن ما ذكرت من الأدلة يوجب بطلان القول بالجهة، لما في إثباتها من إثبات أمارات الحدوث، فما ذكرت من الدليل يوجب القول بالجهة لما في الامتناع عن القول به إثبات عدمه، وكما لا يجوز إثبات حدوث ما ثبت قدمه بالدليل، لا يجوز نفي ما ثبت وجوده بالدليل.
And the Karrami may say: What you mentioned of proofs necessitates the invalidity of the statement about direction, because in affirming it there is an affirmation of signs of temporality (ḥudūth). So what you mentioned of proof necessitates the statement about direction because in refraining from stating it there is an affirmation of its non-existence. And just as it is not permissible to affirm the temporality of what has been established as eternal by proof, it is not permissible to negate what has been established as existent by proof.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the Karramiyya (a sect that affirmed direction for God). They argue that the author's proof against direction is self-defeating. The proof, they say, shows that affirming direction would imply that God is temporal (ḥudūth). But, the Karrami says, the opposite is also true: denying direction would imply that God is non-existent. Both claims rely on proof. And just as we cannot use proof to declare eternal what is proven to be temporal, we also cannot use proof to negate what has been proven to exist. Therefore, the author's proof cannot be used to negate direction if direction is proven by another proof.

Key Terms
كرامي
جهة
حدوث
قدم
دليل
نفي
إثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karrami objection is a denial of proof altogether.
Correction: The objection is not against proof per se, but against an inconsistent application of proof: if proof can negate direction, it can also prove it.

Checkpoint
What is the Karrami's main point in this objection?
A) Direction is proven by consensus.
B) The proof against direction also proves direction because negation implies non-existence.
C) The author's proof is invalid.
D) Direction is necessary for existence.

Key Takeaway
The Karrami objection asserts that rejecting direction based on proof is inconsistent if the same proof can also establish direction through negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth because accepting it would require changing your worldview? How does the Karrami's insistence on consistency challenge you to examine your own reasoning about God? Will you commit to applying the same standard of proof to all aspects of your faith, even when it leads to uncomfortable conclusions?
?
Are there areas in your life where you apply a strict proof for others' beliefs but a lenient one for your own? What would the Karrami say about that inconsistency? Resolve today to hold your own beliefs to the same logical standard you demand of others.
?
The principle 'just as... so also...' is a call to fairness. Where in your daily decisions—not just theology—do you fail to treat similar situations similarly? Ask Allah for the courage to live consistently, and make one concrete change today.

Reports of Jahm's View and the Objection from Verses
Reports conflicting views of Jahm and presents the objection from verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conflicting reports about Jahm's view on God's knowledge of the non-existent and the Qur'anic objection raised against it.

Core Idea
The objection against God knowing the non-existent relies on misinterpreting Qur'anic verses.

In this passage, the author presents two conflicting reports about Jahm's stance on divine knowledge of the non-existent, then lays out the objection from those who deny such knowledge, using verses that seem to imply God acquires knowledge through events. This sets the stage for the refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the two reports about Jahm and the list of verses used in the objection.
The first part of the passage deals with reports, the second with objection.
2
Identify the two reports
The passage reports that Jahm said God knows things only at their occurrence, and also that He knows them before by a created knowledge. Which is considered more reliable?
The author states the first report is more confirmed from their masters.
3
Analyze the objection from verses
Look at each verse cited: testing, qibla, hopeful language, seeing, and determining parties. The objectors argue these imply God acquires knowledge at the time of the event.
The objection is not the author's own view; it is presented to be refuted later.
4
Understand the core difference
The objection claims that God does not know the non-existent, only what occurs. This challenges the established doctrine that God's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محال وروى أبو الحسين الخياط عن جهم أيضا أنه كان يقول: إن الله تعالى يعلم الشيء في حالة حدوثه، وأحال العلم بالمعدوم. لكن ما روينا من الرواية عنه أثبت فإن مشايخنا كذا رووا عنه. وذكر عبد القاهر البغدادي أن جماعة من المتكلمين حكوا عنه أنه قال: إن الله تعالى يعلم الأشياء قبل حدوثها بعلم أحدثه قبلها. وهذا نص أنه كان يجوز كون المعدوم معلوما. وتعلق من زعم أن الله تعالى لا يعلم المعدوم، وإنما يعلم ما حدث عنه حدوثه بقوله: {يبلوكم أيكم أحسن عملا} والابتلاء إنما يكون ليظهر ما لم يكن ظاهرا، وتحصيل علم لم يكن حاصلا بحال من ابتلاه به. ولقوله تعالى: {وما جعلنا القبلة التي كنت عليها إلا لنعلم من يتبع الرسول ممن ينقلب على عقبيه} ولقوله تعالى: {لعله يتذكر أو يخشى} واستعمال لفظة الترجي ممن كان عالما بالأمر محال. وقد قال تعالى: {لننظر كيف تعملون} وقال تعالى: {لنعلم أي الحزبين أحصى لما لبثوا أمدا} وغير ذلك من الآيات وهي تدل كلها على أنه لا يكون عالما بالمعدوم ما لم يوجد.
Impossible. And Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat narrated from Jahm also that he used to say: Indeed God knows the thing at the moment of its occurrence, and he deemed knowledge of the non-existent impossible. But what we have narrated from him is more confirmed, for our masters thus narrated from him. And 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi mentioned that a group of theologians reported from him that he said: Indeed God knows things before their occurrence with a knowledge He brings into existence before them. And this is a clear text that he allowed the non-existent to be known. And those who claim that God does not know the non-existent, but rather knows what occurs from it at its occurrence, have clung to His saying: 'To test you which of you is best in deed' (67:2) – and testing is only to manifest what was not apparent, and to acquire knowledge that was not obtained by the one who tests him – and to His saying: 'And We made not the qibla that you were on except that We know who follows the messenger from him who turns back on his heels' (2:143), and to His saying: 'Perhaps he will remember or fear' (20:44) – and the use of the expression of hope by one who knows the matter is impossible – and He said: 'That We might see how you act' (10:14), and He said: 'That We know which of the two parties is more precise in counting the time they tarried' (18:12), and other verses, all indicating that He is not knowing of the non-existent until it exists.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage reports two conflicting views attributed to Jahm: one that God only knows things at the moment of their occurrence, denying knowledge of the non-existent, and another that He knows things before they occur by a created knowledge. The author notes that the first view is more reliable from their masters. Then the passage presents the objection of those who deny knowledge of the non-existent, using Qur'anic verses that seem to imply that God acquires knowledge through events, such as testing to manifest what was hidden, and the use of hopeful language like 'perhaps'. These verses are taken out of context by the objectors. The objection is that God cannot know the non-existent because if He did, such expressions would be meaningless. The next lesson will provide the refutation.

Key Terms
جهم
العلم بالمعدوم
الابتلاء
الترجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic verses about testing, hope, and seeing prove that God does not have knowledge of future events.
Correction: The objectors misinterpret these verses. The verses use human language for relational purposes; God's eternal knowledge is not contingent on events.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against God's knowledge of the non-existent based on Qur'anic verses?
A) The Qur'an does not mention the non-existent.
B) Verses about testing and hope imply that God acquires knowledge at the time of events.
C) The theologians do not agree on the meaning of the verses.
D) It is against reason to say God knows the non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The objection from Qur'anic verses is a misinterpretation that the author will refute, as it contradicts the established doctrine of God's eternal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the objection that God's testing implies He does not already know the outcome, do you feel any hidden doubt about the completeness of His knowledge? What does that doubt reveal about your understanding of God's attributes? Commit now to seeking clarity from this lesson's refutation.
?
Have you ever used a Qur'anic verse to justify a limited view of God, even unintentionally? Which verse? What step will you take today to ensure you interpret the Qur'an in a way that upholds God's perfection?
?
If you truly believed that God knows everything, including your actions before you do them, how would your sense of personal accountability change? Pray to Allah to grant you certainty in His eternal knowledge and act on that certainty today.

Mu'tazila Denial of Attributes: Two Key Arguments
Author presents Mu'tazila position that Allah has no attributes and their two main shubuh (suspicions).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position that Allah has no attributes and their two main arguments (shubuh) against affirming divine attributes.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that affirming attributes leads to multiple eternal beings, violating tawhid.

In this passage, the author presents the Mu'tazila claim that Allah has no attributes such as life, knowledge, power, hearing, or sight. He then explains two of their doubts (shubuh) that aim to show that affirming attributes would contradict the unity of Allah (tawhid). This lesson will walk through these arguments and help you understand the Mu'tazila theological stance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Read the opening of the passage where the author states the Mu'tazila claim that Allah has no attributes. Notice the paradoxical phrasing: 'living without life, knowing without knowledge.'
This sets the stage for why they need arguments (shubuh) to defend such a claim.
2
Understand the first shubha
The first argument: if an attribute is not identical to the essence, it must be other than the essence (like Zayd vs 'Amr). Affirming multiple distinct things in eternity contradicts tawhid.
This is the 'otherness' argument.
3
Understand the second shubha
The second argument: if attributes are real, they must be eternal. Pre-eternity (qidam) is unique to Allah, so affirming eternal attributes would imply multiple gods.
This is the 'pre-eternity implies divinity' argument.
4
Note the incomplete third shubha
The passage begins a third argument about the everlastingness (baqa') of attributes but cuts off. Be aware that the author's full refutation is not included here.
The source window is incomplete for the third shubha.
5
Reflect on the author's attitude
Notice the author introduces these as 'ignorance' and 'suspicions'. He clearly rejects them, but his refutation is not in this passage. Consider why these arguments might be considered flawed.
This prepares you for the refutation that follows in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت المعتزلة: أن الله تعالى لا حياة له ولا قدرة ولا علم ولا سمع ولا بصر. فهو حي لا حياة له عالم لا علم له، وكذا في الصفات كلها. والذي دعاهم إلى هذا التجاهل شبه منها: أن الصفة إذا لم تكن هي الذات لا محالة فهي غير الذات لا محالة. كزيد لما لم يكن عمرًا كان غير عمرو لا محالة، والقول بإثبات الأشياء المتغايرة في الأزل مناف للتوحيد. ولهذا سموا أنفسهم أهل التوحيد. ومنها أن هذه الصفات لو كان ثابتة لكانت أزلية. إذ القول بحدوث الصفات للقديم محال، وإذا كانت أزلية كانت قديمة، والقدم هو الوصف الخاص الله تعالى فكان القول بثبوت الصفات في الأزل قولا بثبوت الآلهة لإثبات معنى الألوهية وهو القدم للصفات، والقول بالآلهة مناف للتوحيد. ومنها أن الصفات لو ثبتت لكانت باقية، ضرورة، لا وجه إلى القول ببقاء كل
And the Mu'tazila claimed that Allah, exalted, has no life, no power, no knowledge, no hearing, and no sight. So He is living without life, knowing without knowledge, and likewise for all attributes. What led them to this ignorance are suspicions (shubuh), among them: that if the attribute is not the essence, necessarily it is other than the essence, just as Zayd when he is not 'Amr is necessarily other than 'Amr. And affirming multiple distinct things in eternity contradicts tawhid. For this reason they called themselves 'people of tawhid'. And among them: that if these attributes were established, they would be eternal; for affirming temporal origination of attributes for the Eternal is impossible. And if they are eternal, they are pre-eternal (qadima). And pre-eternity (al-qidam) is the specific description of Allah, so affirming the establishment of attributes in eternity is a statement affirming gods (aliha) because it proves the meaning of divinity (al-uluhiyya), which is pre-eternity, for the attributes. And the affirmation of gods contradicts tawhid. And among them: that if attributes were established, they would necessarily be everlasting (baqiya); there is no way to affirm the everlastingness of every
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila position and their arguments against divine attributes. The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazila deny that Allah has life, power, knowledge, hearing, or sight; they claim He is living without life, knowing without knowledge, etc. Then he presents two of their doubts (shubuh). The first argument: if an attribute is not identical to the essence, it must be other than the essence, just as Zayd is other than 'Amr. Affirming multiple distinct entities in eternity (like the attributes) would violate tawhid. Hence they call themselves 'people of tawhid'. The second argument: if these attributes were real, they would be eternal (since temporal origination is impossible for the Eternal). Being eternal, they would be pre-eternal (qadima). But pre-eternity is unique to Allah; so affirming eternal attributes would mean affirming multiple gods, because pre-eternity is the defining quality of divinity. Thus it contradicts tawhid. The text then begins a third doubt about the attributes' everlastingness (baqa'), but cuts off. The refutation from the author's perspective is not included in this passage.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
صفات (sifāt)
توحيد (tawḥīd)
أزل (azal)
قدم (qidam)
ألوهية (ulūhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Mu'tazila arguments are logically valid and provide a strong rational case against divine attributes.
Correction: The author presents these as 'shubuh' (suspicions/fallacies) arising from ignorance. The arguments are considered flawed because they misunderstand the relationship between Allah's essence and attributes; later refutations show that attributes are not separate entities but are identical to the essence in a way that does not compromise tawhid.

Checkpoint
What is the first doubt (shubha) of the Mu'tazila against divine attributes?
A) Attributes are created and thus cannot be eternal
B) If attributes are not the essence, they must be other, implying multiple eternals
C) Attributes are unknowable and cannot be affirmed
D) Attributes would make Allah composite

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila attempt to defend tawhid by denying attributes, arguing that affirming attributes separate from the essence would multiply eternal beings and thus violate God's unique oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been so zealous to protect a core belief (like tawhid) that you rejected a truth about Allah that is clearly revealed? What was it, and how did you justify it to yourself?
?
The Mu'tazila used logical reasoning that seemed airtight, yet it led them to strip Allah of His attributes. Where in your own life have you trusted your intellect over the clear texts, only to realize later you were wrong?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah knows you in detail (not just a vague knowledge), how would it change the way you live today? Are there areas where you unconsciously act as if He is 'knowing without knowledge'?

Nasafi's Critique of Abu Hashim's Mu'tazilite Doctrine
Nasafi criticizes Abu Hashim's intellectual dishonesty and doctrinal contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the Mu'tazilite denial of God's attributes, which they call tawhid, actually contradicts tawhid and aligns with dualism.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite 'tawhid' is a cover for dualism.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi delivers a sharp critique of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, the leader of the Mu'tazila. He accuses Abu Hashim of knowingly promoting falsehood by denying God's attributes, exposing a contradiction at the heart of the Mu'tazilite claim to be the people of justice and tawhid. As you read, notice how Nasafi turns their own terminology against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the tone and key accusations.
The passage uses strong polemical language—notice words like 'ضحكة' (laughingstock) and 'باطل' (falsehood).
2
Identify the core accusation
What does Nasafi accuse Abu Hashim of doing? He says Abu Hashim 'did not care about disengaging from tawhid and approving dualism' (الانسلاخ عن التوحيد وتصويب الثنوية).
3
Connect to the doctrinal contradiction
Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazilite denial of attributes (which they call tawhid) actually requires feigned ignorance (تجاهل) and leads to dualism (ثنوية). How does this refute their claim to be the people of justice and tawhid?
Consider the phrase 'لا يوصل إليه إلا بالتجاهل' — their justice is only reached through feigned ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأى أبي هاشم الجبائي] فلما انتهت نوبة رئاسة المعتزلة إلى أبي هاشم ، ورأى سلفه بذلك دينه وعقله في نصرة مذهب المعتزلة، ولم يبال عن التجاهل، وتصيير نفسه ضحكة للخلق في ترويج ما هم عليه من الباطل في نفي الصفات الذي هو عندهم توحيد، كما لم يبال عن الانسلاخ عن التوحيد وتصويب الثنوية في مسائل التعديل والتجوير التي هي ،، ويسمون أنفسهم أهل عدل وتوحيد لا يوصل إليه إلا بالتجاهل وعدل العدل، عندهم لا يوصل إليه إلا بإبطال التوحيد وتصويب الثنوية، لقليل فخر من تمسك بها، كثير مثالب من دان بهما ظاهر فساد عقيدة من اعتقدهما، ونسأل الله العصمة عن الضلال، والخروج عن مقتضى العقول وموجب ما يصح من الأصول لعمى يعترينا ، وصمم خالطنا لحب الباطل والميل بهو انا إلى الظلال.
The opinion of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i. When the turn of leadership of the Mu'tazila came to Abu Hashim, and he saw his predecessors [employing] that as their religion and intellect in supporting the Mu'tazilite doctrine, he did not care about feigning ignorance, and making himself a laughingstock for the people in promoting the falsehood they were upon in negating the attributes, which according to them is tawhid (monotheism), just as he did not care about disengaging from tawhid and approving dualism in the matters of al-ta'dil wa al-tajwir (declaring God just and unjust) which are... And they call themselves the people of justice and tawhid, yet it is not reached except through feigned ignorance, and the justice of justice according to them is not reached except by nullifying tawhid and approving dualism. Little pride for one who clings to it, many defects for one who holds them. The corruption of the creed of one who believes them is apparent. And we ask God for protection from misguidance, and from going against the dictates of intellects and what is required by sound principles due to blindness that afflicts us and deafness that mixes with us from love of falsehood and inclination, woe to us, toward darkness.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a polemical critique of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, the Mu'tazilite leader. Nasafi accuses Abu Hashim of knowingly promoting falsehood by denying God's attributes, which the Mu'tazila consider tawhid (monotheism). He argues that this denial actually leads to contradicting tawhid and agreeing with dualism (thunawiya), especially in issues of divine justice (ta'dil and tajwir). Nasafi points out the hypocrisy of calling themselves 'people of justice and tawhid' while their doctrines undermine both. He concludes with a prayer for protection from misguidance and from the love of falsehood that leads to darkness. The passage serves as a strong condemnation of the Mu'tazilite position on attributes.

Key Terms
أبو هاشم
معتزلة
تجاهل
توحيد
ثنوية
تعديل
تجوير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite denial of God's attributes is true tawhid (monotheism).
Correction: Nasafi argues that this denial actually leads to contradicting tawhid and approving dualism (thunawiya), especially in matters of divine justice.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi accuse Abu Hashim of doing regarding tawhid?
A) Defending tawhid properly
B) Rejecting tawhid and embracing dualism
C) Being consistent with the Salaf
D) Promoting true monotheism

Key Takeaway
Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazilite claim to tawhid as self-contradictory because their denial of attributes requires feigned ignorance and ultimately leads to dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever supported a belief or group you knew was false just to maintain your position or leadership? Reflect on Abu Hashim, who Nasafi says made himself a laughingstock for the sake of promoting falsehood. In what area of your life are you tempted to pretend ignorance to hold onto status or comfort?
?
Nasafi prays for protection from misguidance due to 'love of falsehood'. Where in your own life do you love a falsehood—perhaps a comfortable but incorrect belief about yourself, others, or God? What concrete step will you take today to expose that falsehood to the truth?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed to be 'people of justice and tawhid' but their doctrine contradicted both. Is there a claim you make about yourself (e.g., 'I am a person of faith') that your actual choices contradict? What would it cost you to align your claims with reality?

Critique of Abu Hashim's Approach to Divine Attributes
Censure of Abu Hashim's method and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author critiques Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i's method of denying divine attributes and why he considers it a contradiction to true tawhid.

Core Idea
Denying attributes to affirm tawhid can lead to dualism and self-contradiction.

This passage is a direct polemic against Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazilite leader. The author argues that the Mu'tazilite denial of divine attributes, which they consider tawhid, actually undermines tawhid and resembles dualism. As you read, notice how the author contrasts their self-proclaimed title with the logical consequences of their doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the author's tone and argument. Note the strong language used.
The author uses terms like 'feigning ignorance' and 'laughingstock'.
2
Identify the main critique
Find what the author accuses Abu Hashim of: promoting falsehood, disregarding reason, and contradicting tawhid.
3
Analyze the self-contradiction
Notice how the author points out that the Mu'tazila call themselves 'People of Justice and Tawhid' yet their doctrine requires nullifying tawhid and approving dualism.
This is a key polemical point.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes with a prayer for protection from such misguidance. Consider what this reveals about the author's perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما انتهت نوبة رئاسة المعتزلة إلى أبي هاشم، ورأى سلفه بذلك دينه وعقله في نصرة مذهب المعتزلة، ولم يبال عن التجاهل، وتصيير نفسه ضحكة للخلق في ترويج ما هم عليه من الباطل في نفي الصفات الذي هو عندهم توحيد، كما لم يبال عن الانسلاخ عن التوحيد وتصويب الثنوية في مسائل التعديل والتجوير التي هي، ويسمون أنفسهم أهل عدل وتوحيد لا يوصل إليه إلا بالتجاهل وعدل العدل، عندهم لا يوصل إليه إلا بإبطال التوحيد وتصويب الثنوية، لقليل فخر من تمسك بها، كثير مثالب من دان بهما ظاهر فساد عقيدة من اعتقدهما، ونسأل الله العصمة عن الضلال، والخروج عن مقتضى العقول وموجب ما يصح من الأصول لعمى يعترينا، وصمم خالطنا لحب الباطل والميل بهوانا إلى الظلال.
When the turn of leadership of the Mu'tazila came to Abu Hashim, and he saw his predecessors [treat] thereby their religion and reason in supporting the Mu'tazilite doctrine, he did not care about feigning ignorance, and making himself a laughingstock for people in promoting the falsehood they hold in negating the attributes, which for them is tawhid. Likewise, he did not care about stripping himself of tawhid and approving dualism in matters of justice and injustice—and they call themselves 'People of Justice and Tawhid'—yet it is not attained except by feigning ignorance; and justice itself, in their view, is not attained except by nullifying tawhid and approving dualism. Little pride for one who clings to them, many defects for one who adheres to them—manifest corruption of the creed of whoever believes them. We ask God for protection from misguidance, and from departing from the dictates of intellects and what is required by sound principles, due to the blindness that afflicts us and the deafness that has mixed with us from love of falsehood and inclination through our lowliness to darkness.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a polemical critique of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, a leader of the Mu'tazila. The author condemns Abu Hashim for knowingly ignoring reason and making himself ridiculous to promote the Mu'tazilite denial of divine attributes, which they mistakenly call tawhid. The author further criticizes their self-designation as 'People of Justice and Tawhid', arguing that their concept of justice actually undermines tawhid and resembles dualism. The passage ends with a prayer for protection from such misguidance.

Correction: The author argues that their denial of attributes actually leads to dualism and contradicts true tawhid, making their self-designation as 'People of Justice and Tawhid' hypocritical.

According to the author, what do the Mu'tazila call themselves?
A) People of Unity
B) People of Justice and Tawhid
C) People of Attributes
D) People of Reason

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite denial of divine attributes, despite their claim to uphold tawhid, is seen by the author as a corruption that leads to dualism and self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been so committed to a belief that you ignored its contradictions, just as the Mu'tazila claimed tawhid while embracing dualism? Search your heart for any inconsistency in your own understanding of Allah's attributes—are you truly affirming them as He deserves? Commit to examining one belief this week that might be built on your own logic rather than revelation.
?
The author accuses Abu Hashim of feigning ignorance to promote falsehood. Is there an area of your faith where you have knowingly looked away from a truth because admitting it would demand change? What would it cost you to embrace that truth fully, and are you willing to pay it? Pray for the courage to face the truth even if it overturns your comfort.
?
The Mu'tazila called themselves 'People of Justice and Tawhid' yet their path led to the corruption of both. Do you carry any label or identity—Islamic or otherwise—that you hold dear, while deep down you know your actions or beliefs betray that name? Write down one such gap and take a concrete step today to close it.

The Foundational Contradiction in the Mu'tazilite Theory of States
Nasafi poses rhetorical questions and presents the first horn of a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial dilemma Nasafi poses: if the Mu'tazilite claims the state is neither essence nor meaning, how can it be mentioned or known, and what are the consequences of equating the witnessed and unseen.

Core Idea
Claiming an unknown state to explain knowledge is self-defeating.

Nasafi begins by addressing the Mu'tazilite who posits an attribute (hal) that is neither identical to the essence nor a separate meaning. He points out a logical flaw: if the state is not mentioned or known, how can one use it to explain how the essence is knowing? This leads to a dilemma about whether to equate the witnessed and unseen realms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Nasafi uses rhetorical questions to expose the contradiction.
Focus on the questions: 'If the state is not mentioned, how did you mention it?'
2
Identify the dilemma
Nasafi presents two options: either equate the witnessed and unseen, or do not. Identify where these options are stated.
The two options start after 'أنت بين أمرين'.
3
Understand the first option
If the Mu'tazilite equates, then the state requires knowledge and knowledge requires the state, leading to agreement with Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox). See why this undermines the necessity of a separate 'hal'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does equating lead to agreement? Because it makes knowledge (a real attribute) sufficient, eliminating the need for the problematic 'hal'.
This is the core of Nasafi's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وراء الذات، فقد انقدت للحق، ولم يجد لك التجاهل نفعا، وإن قلت: ليس راجعة إلى الذات ولا إلى معنى وراء الذات، فقد أحلت إذ أثبت واسطة بين الذات وبين ما وراء الذات. ثم يقال له: إذا لم يكن الحال مذكورة فكيف ذكرتها؟ وإذا ذكرتها فكيف زعمت أنها ليست بمذكورة؟ وإذا لم تعلمها كيف علمت أن الذات عالم وهو لا يكون عالما إلا بالحال؟ وإذا لم يعلم الحال كيف علم الذات عالما؟ وإذا علمت كيف زعمت أنها ليست بمعلومة؟ ثم يقال: إن في الشاهد كانت الحال عندك من مقتضيات العلم، وكذا في كل صفة، واتصاف الذات بكونه عالم من مقتضيات الحال، فبعد هذا فأنت بين أمرين: إما أن تسوي بين الشاهد والغائب فلا يثبت الحال إلا بإثبات ما يقتضيها وهو العلم كما لا يثبت العلم إلا بإثبات ما يقتضيه وهو الحال، فيصير موافقا لأهل الحق في إثبات العلم، ولم يقع لك إلى إثبات الحال التي ليست بمعلومة حاجة. وإما إن كنت لا تسوي بين الشاهد والغائب فيثبت الحال بدون ما يقتضيها، فتصير مناقضا من وجهين: أحدهما: إنك لم تثبت كونه عالما بدون ما يقتضيه وهو الحال وتثبت الحال بدون ما يقتضيها وهذه مناقضة. فمن حقك إذا جوزت ثبوت الحال بدون ما يقتضيها وهو العلم أن تجوز به ثبوت الاتصاف بكونه عالما بدون ما يقتضيه وهو الحال فتلتحق بسلفك ويلزمك ما يلزمهم. أو لا يجوز ثبوت الحال بدون ما يقتضيها، كما لا يجوز ثبوت الاتصاف بكونه عالما بدون ما يقتضيه وهو الحال فتلتحق بأهل الحق والله الموفق. والمناقضة الثانية: أنك لم تجوز إثبات الحال التي بها يصير الذات مريدا في الغائب بدون ما يقتضيها وهو الإرادة، وكذا الحال التي يصير بها متكلما في الغائب بدون الكلام، تسوية بين الشاهد والغائب، فلا يجوز أيضا إثبات الحال التي بها يصير الذات عالما، والحال التي يصير بها قادرا، وكذا الحال التي يصير بها باقيا سميعا بصيرا، إلا بإثبات ما يقتضيها، وإلا فالفرق وليس لك إلى الفرق سبيل. وجوز أيضا في الغائب الحال التي بها يصير الذات متحركا أو ساكنا، وكذا في جميع الأكوان والألوان والطعوم والروائح، من غير ثبوت ما يقتضيها، وإلا فافرق. وهذا الكلام يغني سماعه عن الاشتغال برده، ولولا اتباع أكثر معتزلة زماننا رأي هذا الملحد في ضلالاته، وإلا لما أشبعنا في إفساد هذا الكلام كل هذا الإشباع، لاستغنائنا عن ذلك بشهادة البداءة ببطلانه والتحاق قائله بالمتجاهلة. والله الموفق.
Beyond the essence, you have submitted to the truth, and ignorance has no benefit for you. And if you say: it is not referable to the essence nor to a meaning beyond the essence, then you have contradicted yourself since you posited an intermediary between the essence and what is beyond the essence. Then it is said to him: If the state is not mentioned, how did you mention it? And if you mentioned it, how do you claim that it is not mentioned? And if you do not know it, how did you know that the essence is knowing, and it is only knowing through the state? And if the state is not known, how did you know the essence to be knowing? And if you know it, how do you claim that it is not known? Then it is said: In the witnessed, the state according to you is a requirement of knowledge, and likewise in every attribute, and the description of the essence as being knowing is a requirement of the state. After this, you are between two matters: Either you equate between the witnessed and the unseen, so the state is not established except by establishing what requires it, which is knowledge, just as knowledge is not established except by establishing what requires it, which is the state, and you become in agreement with the people of truth in establishing knowledge, and you have no need to establish the state which is not known. Or you do not equate between the witnessed and the unseen, so you establish the state without what requires it, and you become contradictory in two respects: One of them: You do not establish His being knowing without what requires it, which is the state, and you establish the state without what requires it, and this is a contradiction. So it is incumbent upon you, if you permit the existence of the state without what requires it, which is knowledge, to permit thereby the existence of description as being knowing without what requires it, which is the state, and you join your predecessors and what applies to them applies to you. Or you do not permit the existence of the state without what requires it, just as you do not permit the existence of description as being knowing without what requires it, which is the state, and you join the people of truth, and God is the suporter. The second contradiction: You do not permit establishing the state by which the essence becomes willing in the unseen without what requires it, which is will, and likewise the state by which it becomes speaking in the unseen without speech, as an equation between the witnessed and the unseen. So you may not permit establishing the state by which the essence becomes knowing, and the state by which it becomes powerful, and likewise the state by which it becomes everlasting, hearing, seeing, except by establishing what requires them; otherwise there is a difference and you have no way to differentiate. And you also permit in the unseen the state by which the essence becomes moving or stationary, and likewise in all the modes, colors, tastes, and smells, without establishing what requires them; otherwise differentiate. And this statement is such that hearing it suffices from occupying oneself with refuting it, and were it not for the following of most of the Mu'tazila of our time for the opinion of this heretic in his errors, we would not have exhausted ourselves in corrupting this statement with all this elaboration, for we are independent of that by the testimony of the beginning of its falsehood and the attachment of its speaker to the ignorant. And God is the suporter.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite concept of 'al-hal' (state). The author, Imam al-Nasafi, first addresses an opponent who posits an attribute (hal) that is neither identical to the essence nor entirely distinct. He points out the contradiction: if the state is not mentioned, how can they mention it? If they claim it is unknown, how can they use it to explain knowledge? He then presents a dilemma: either one equates the witnessed and the unseen, in which case the state requires knowledge just as knowledge requires the state, leading to agreement with Ahl al-Haqq; or one does not equate them, leading to two contradictions. The first contradiction is that they do not establish the attribute of being knowing without the state, yet they establish the state without knowledge, which is inconsistent. The second contradiction is that they inconsistently allow some states (like being moving) without their requirements in the unseen, while disallowing others (like being willing). The author concludes that the argument is self-evidently false and that only following a heretic would lead to its acceptance.

Key Terms
الحال
الشاهد
الغائب
مذكورة
معلومة
مقتضيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite 'hal' is a knowable intermediate entity that explains attributes without being an attribute itself.
Correction: Nasafi argues that if the 'hal' is not known, it cannot be used as an explanation; and if it is known, it falls into the category of known attributes, making it redundant.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the consequence if the Mu'tazilite equates the witnessed and unseen in the theory of states?
A) The state becomes unknowable.
B) The state is established only with knowledge, making the opponent agree with Ahl al-Haqq.
C) The essence becomes identical to the state.
D) The state becomes a separate eternal entity.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's first line of refutation: if the state is neither known nor mentioned, it cannot explain divine knowledge; equating leads to agreement with orthodoxy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used an unknown concept (like 'we don't know how') to avoid submitting to a clear divine attribute from the Quran and Sunnah? What would it cost you to simply affirm what Allah has said about Himself without explaining it away?
?
If your reasoning about Allah's attributes leads to a contradiction (as with the Mu'tazilites), are you willing to abandon that reasoning and accept the orthodox position, even if it feels less intellectually satisfying? When has your pride in your own intellect stopped you from accepting a straightforward truth?
?
The passage says 'ignorance has no benefit for you.' Where in your life are you using ignorance as an excuse to remain comfortable in a practice or belief that deep down you know contradicts revelation? Will you seek knowledge today to remove that ignorance, or will you cling to it?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate

The Two Contradictions Arising from Not Equating the Witnessed and Unseen
Nasafi enumerates two contradictions and dismisses the theory as self-evidently false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two specific contradictions Nasafi identifies when the Mu'tazilite does not equate the witnessed and unseen in their theory of states.

Core Idea
Inconsistently applying the state theory to some attributes but not others leads to absurdity.

Having presented the first horn of the dilemma, Nasafi now considers the second: if the Mu'tazilite does not equate the witnessed and unseen, they fall into two clear contradictions. The first concerns establishing the state without its requirement (knowledge), and the second involves selectively applying the theory to different attributes, including accidental qualities like motion and color.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Begin with 'Ima in kunta la tusawi...' and read to the end. Notice how Nasafi lists two contradictions (مناقضتان).
The first contradiction is about the relationship between state and knowledge.
2
Understand the first contradiction
Nasafi argues that if the opponent does not equate, they establish the state without what requires it (knowledge), but they do not establish being knowing without the state. This is inconsistent.
The opponent must either accept both or neither.
3
Understand the second contradiction
The opponent allows some states (like being willing, speaking) without their requirements in the unseen, but disallows others (like being knowing, powerful). They also allow accidental states (moving, colors) without requirements.
This shows arbitrary discrimination.
4
Consider the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the argument is self-evidently false and only followed by heretics.
Reflect on why this dismissive tone is used.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite concept of 'al-hal' (state). The author, Imam al-Nasafi, first addresses an opponent who posits an attribute (hal) that is neither identical to the essence nor entirely distinct. He points out the contradiction: if the state is not mentioned, how can they mention it? If they claim it is unknown, how can they use it to explain knowledge? He then presents a dilemma: either one equates the witnessed and the unseen, in which case the state requires knowledge just as knowledge requires the state, leading to agreement with Ahl al-Haqq; or one does not equate them, leading to two contradictions. The second contradiction is that they inconsistently allow some states (like being moving) without their requirements in the unseen, while disallowing others (like being willing). The author concludes that the argument is self-evidently false and that only following a heretic would lead to its acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite theory of states can be consistently applied to all divine attributes and accidental qualities.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the theory is applied inconsistently: states for some attributes require their underlying realities, while for others they do not, and accidental states are allowed without any requirement.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the second contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position when they do not equate the witnessed and unseen?
A) They allow states for willing and speaking without requirements, but disallow states for knowing and power without requirements.
B) They deny the existence of states altogether.
C) They claim states are identical to the essence.
D) They establish knowledge without the state.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite theory of states leads to unresolved contradictions and arbitrary distinctions, making it untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you approach Islamic theology, are you selectively applying principles to fit your desires, just as the Mu'tazilites selectively applied the state theory? What attribute of Allah are you most uncomfortable with, and how does that discomfort reveal a lack of submission?
?
Nasafi says that if the Mu'tazilite permits the state without requirement, they join the heretics; if they deny both, they join Ahl al-Haqq. Are you willing to accept the full implications of affirming Allah's attributes without reservation, even if your mind cannot fully grasp them? Or do you try to carve out a middle ground that feels comfortable?
?
The second contradiction shows that we sometimes allow for some attributes what we deny for others. In your life, do you hold others to standards that you do not apply to yourself? How does this passage expose the danger of intellectual inconsistency in your own faith?

Refuting the Intuitive Knowledge Claim
The author refutes the Mu'tazila's claim that knowledge of the agent being knowing is intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the knowledge that an agent is knowing cannot be intuitive, as argued by the author against the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Knowledge built on acquired knowledge cannot itself be intuitive.

The Mu'tazila, specifically the followers of al-Ka'bi, claimed that when we see a perfect act, we intuitively know that its agent is knowing. The author refutes this, arguing that such knowledge is impossible because it rests on knowledge of the agent, which is acquired through inference. He then exposes a contradiction in their position, showing that they simultaneously claim the knowledge is intuitive and that it cannot occur before inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to grasp the author's argument.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'بديهيات' (intuitive) and 'مكتسب' (acquired).
2
Understand the claim being refuted
Identify what the followers of al-Ka'bi are claiming: that knowledge of the agent being knowing is intuitive.
This is the starting point of the refutation.
3
Follow the logical argument
The author argues that knowledge of the agent being knowing depends on knowledge of the agent, which itself is acquired through inference. Therefore, the dependent knowledge cannot be intuitive.
Notice the chain: knowledge of agent (acquired) → knowledge of agent being knowing (built on that).
4
Identify the contradiction
The author points out that the Ka'bis claim both that the knowledge is intuitive (occurring at the moment of perception) and that it is impossible for such necessary knowledge to occur before inference. This is a manifest contradiction.
This contradiction undermines their entire position.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author labels such a claim as foolish and impossible. Consider why this refutation is important for establishing that theological knowledge requires inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال لأصحاب الكعبي : إن دعوى كون العلم بكون الفاعل عالما من باب البديهيات دعوى ممنوعة بل هي من جملة المستحيلات، فإن العلم بكونه عالما منبئ على العلم بكونه فاعلا، والعلم بالفاعل بدلالة الفعل المحكم على مكتسب بمساعدة الكعبي إيانا إذ المعارف ليست بضرورية، وإذا كان العالم بثبوت الفاعل للمفعول مكتسبا، كيف يكون العلم بما هو مبني عليه ضروريا بديهيا فإن من زعم أن العلم المبني على العلم المكتسب ضروري - وإن كان لا يتوصل إليه إلا بالتوصل إلى الأول، ولن يتوصل إلى الأول إلا بالاستدلال وصار هو بهذه الواسطة مستحيل الحصول إلا بالاكتساب والاستدلال - كان متحامقا ، وهذا لأنه جعل المعرفة لمن عاين المفعول المحكم المتقن بكون فاعله عالما قادرا ثابتة بطريق الضرورة حال ما عاين قبل حصول الاستدلال الذي يحصل له به المعرفة بالفاعل، ثم زعم أن من المحال ثبوت المعرفة الضرورية قبل هذا الاستدلال، وهذا هو التناقض الظاهر الذي يستنكف عن ركوب مثله من لم يخل من هذا العلم بطائل. والله الموفق.
It is said to the followers of al-Ka'bi: The claim that knowledge of the agent being knowing is from the category of intuitive matters is a prohibited claim, rather it is among the impossibilities. For knowledge that he is knowing indicates knowledge that he is an agent, and knowledge of the agent through the evidence of the perfect act is acquired, with the assistance of al-Ka'bi to us, since cognitions are not necessary. And if the one who knows the establishment of the agent for the effect is acquired, how can knowledge which is built upon it be necessary and intuitive? For whoever claims that knowledge built upon acquired knowledge is necessary - even though it is not reached except by reaching the first, and the first is only reached through inference, and it becomes through this mediation impossible to obtain except by acquisition and inference - is foolish. This is because he makes the recognition for one who perceives the perfect, precise effect that its agent is knowing and powerful, established by way of necessity at the time of perception, before the occurrence of the inference by which he obtains recognition of the agent. Then he claims that it is impossible for necessary knowledge to be established before this inference. This is the manifest contradiction from which anyone who has not been deprived of a share of this knowledge would refrain from committing such. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the followers of al-Ka'bi (a Mu'tazili sub-sect) who argue that knowledge of the agent being knowing is intuitive (badihi). The author first declares this claim prohibited and impossible. The reasoning is that knowledge of the agent being knowing is based on knowledge of the agent (fa'il), and knowledge of the agent through the evidence of the perfect act (fi'l muhkam) is acquired (muktasab), as al-Ka'bi himself admits since cognitions (ma'arif) are not necessary. If the foundational knowledge is acquired, then the knowledge built on it cannot be necessary/intuitive. The author then exposes a contradiction: someone who claims that a person who sees a perfect act immediately knows by necessity that the agent is knowing and powerful before any inference (istidlal) about the agent also claims that it is impossible for such necessary knowledge to occur before the inference. This is a manifest contradiction (tanaqud zahir). The author concludes that such a person is foolish (mutahamiq) and that no reasonable person would commit such an error.

Key Terms
الكعبي
البديهيات
المكتسب
الاستدلال
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that knowledge of the agent being knowing is intuitive (badihi) and does not require inference.
Correction: The author demonstrates that this knowledge is built upon acquired knowledge of the agent, which itself requires inference; therefore, it cannot be intuitive.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for knowledge of the agent being knowing to be intuitive?
A) Because the Mu'tazila reject it.
B) Because it is based on knowledge of the agent which is acquired, so the knowledge built on it cannot be intuitive.
C) Because it is a matter of faith.
D) Because the Ka'bis do not understand it.

Key Takeaway
The knowledge that an agent is knowing cannot be intuitive because it relies on the acquired knowledge of the agent, which is attained through inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the design of the universe, do you assume that your recognition of a Creator is immediate and intuitive, or do you admit that it involves a process of reasoning? How does this passage challenge the comfort of intuitive certainty and call you to a deeper, reflective faith? Will you commit to examining your own theological beliefs to see if they are built on rigorous inference or mere assumption?
?
The author calls the Mu'tazila position 'foolish' and 'a manifest contradiction'. Are there areas in your own faith where you hold two contradictory claims without realizing it? Ask yourself: is your trust in God based on a reasoned conviction that has withstood scrutiny, or on a feeling you have never tested? What would it cost you to honestly examine the foundations of your belief?
?
If all knowledge of God is acquired through inference from His acts, then every moment of reflection becomes an act of worship. When was the last time you deliberately inferred God's attributes from something you observed? This week, choose one perfect act (like your own existence or a sunset) and walk through the steps that lead you to affirm that God is knowing and powerful. Will you do it?

Disagreement Among Rational Thinkers Proves Knowledge is Not Intuitive
The author enumerates differing views of rational groups to prove knowledge of the agent is not intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the disagreement among rational thinkers about the agent of perfect acts proves that knowledge of the agent's attributes is not intuitive but requires inference.

Core Idea
Rational disagreement about the agent proves intuitive knowledge is false.

The author begins by presenting a powerful empirical argument: intelligent people from various schools of thought radically disagree about who or what is responsible for perfect acts. Since they do not disagree about truly intuitive matters, this disagreement shows that knowledge of the agent's attributes cannot be intuitive. Let's read how he uses the views of naturalists, astrologers, Mu'tazila, and Thumama to make his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Then the translation. Notice the list of differing views.
Each group has a different stance on agency.
2
Identify the disagreement
Identify each group mentioned and their different claims about the agent of perfect acts.
Naturalists attribute to natures, astrologers to stars, Mu'tazila to generated acts from inanimate sources, Thumama denies agency.
3
Understand the logic
The author argues that since these rational thinkers agree on intuitive matters but differ here, the knowledge in question cannot be intuitive. Reflect on why this is a strong rebuttal.
Intuitive knowledge is universal; disagreement indicates it's inferential.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Relate this argument to the overall debate about whether Allah's attributes are known intuitively or through inference.
This is part of a larger discourse on causality and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إن كثيرا من أهل الطبائع يجوزون الفعل المحكم من الطبائع، وإن المنجمين منهم من يجوز ذلك من الكواكب، والمعتزلة يجوزون حصول الأفعال المحكمة على طريق التولد عن الميت والنائم والساهي وثمامة بن الأشرس يجعل المتولدات أفعالا لا فاعل لها. وهؤلاء كلهم من أرباب العقول السليمة، لا يختلفون في البديهيات، فدل أن دعوتهم كون العلم بأن الفاعل عالم بديهيا باطلة مجحودة. والله الموفق. ويقال لهم : ما أنكرتم من أن يعكس عليهم فيقال: العلم بأن الفعل المحكم يوجب العلم الضروري بوجود علم فاعله ولا يوجب وجود فاعله عالما إلا بالاستدلال، لما علم أن قيام العلم بذاته من غير ذات يقوم به محال ويقال له: إن كان العلم بثبوت الأعراض استدلاليا ضروريا لاختلاف العقلاء فيه، فكذا لا يعلم كون الفاعل قادرا عالما إلا بالاستدلال، لوقوع الاختلاف فيه بين العقلاء على ما ذكرنا.
Then it is said to them: Indeed many of the naturalists allow the perfect act from natures, and among the astrologers some allow that from the stars, and the Mu'tazila allow the occurrence of perfect acts by way of generation (tawallud) from the dead, the sleeping, and the heedless, and Thumama ibn al-Ashras makes generated acts as acts without an agent. All these are people of sound intellects; they do not differ about intuitive matters. So it shows that their claim that knowledge that the agent is knowing is intuitive is false and rejected. And God is the giver of success. And it is said to them: What prevents you from reversing it against them, so that it is said: Knowledge that the perfect act necessitates necessary knowledge of the existence of the knowledge of its agent, but it does not necessitate the existence of the agent being knowing except by inference, because it is known that the existence of knowledge by itself without a substrate in which it inheres is impossible. And it is said to him: If knowledge of the establishment of accidents is inferential or necessary due to the disagreement of the rational people about it, then likewise the agent is not known to be powerful and knowing except by inference, due to the occurrence of disagreement about it among the rational people as we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage combines two arguments. First, it cites the differing views among rational thinkers: naturalists (ahl al-taba'i') attribute perfect acts to natures, astrologers to stars, the Mu'tazila allow generated acts (mutawallidat) from dead, sleeping, or heedless persons, and Thumama ibn al-Ashras denies any agent for generated acts. All these are people of sound intellect and do not disagree about intuitive matters (badihiyyat). Therefore, the Mu'tazila's claim that knowledge of the agent being knowing is intuitive is false, because intelligent people disagree about it. Second, the author presents a reversal argument: if they claim that the perfect act necessitates necessary knowledge of the existence of the knowledge of its agent, it can be refuted by saying that it does not necessitate that the agent is knowing except by inference, because knowledge cannot subsist by itself. Moreover, if knowledge of accidents is inferential or necessary due to disagreement, then similarly knowledge of the agent's attributes is inferential due to disagreement. This undermines their claim.

Key Terms
أهل الطبائع
المنجمون
التولد
ثمامة بن الأشرس
البديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreement alone cannot disprove intuition; intuition could still be real despite differing opinions.
Correction: The author argues that rational people do not disagree about truly intuitive matters (badihiyyat). Since they disagree about the agent, the knowledge cannot be intuitive.

Checkpoint
Why does the author mention the views of naturalists, astrologers, and Thumama?
A) To show that they are all wrong.
B) To prove that rational people disagree about the agent, so the knowledge is not intuitive.
C) To support the Mu'tazila's position.
D) To argue that knowledge is only through senses.

Key Takeaway
Disagreement among rational thinkers about the agent proves that knowledge of the agent's attributes is not intuitive but inferential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your certainty about God being the agent of all actions might be based on inference rather than intuition? If it were truly intuitive, rational people would not disagree. What does this lesson reveal about the foundation of your own conviction? Will you now seek to strengthen it with proof?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you assume is obvious to every rational person. Now recall someone intelligent who rejects it. Does that disagreement unsettle you? This lesson challenges you to examine whether your belief is truly self-evident or merely inherited. Are you willing to test it against the criteria of intuition?
?
When you encounter a rational person who disagrees with a core tenet of your faith, do you automatically dismiss them as irrational? This lesson shows that even those with 'sound intellects' can differ on non-intuitive matters. How will this change your approach to dialogue? Will you humbly present proofs rather than assume intuition?

The Perfect Act Necessitates Inference, Not Intuition
The author presents a reversal argument and an analogy from disagreement to affirm that knowledge of the agent is inferential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a reversal argument and the nature of knowledge to argue that the agent's attributes can only be known through inference.

Core Idea
Perfect acts necessitate inference, not intuition, because knowledge cannot subsist without a subject.

The author continues the argument by presenting a reversal: even if the perfect act necessitates knowledge of its agent's knowledge, it does not directly make the agent knowing except through inference. This is because knowledge cannot exist by itself without a substrate. Moreover, if knowledge of accidents is inferential due to disagreement, then knowledge of the agent's power and knowledge is also inferential due to the documented disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This passage contains a logical twist.
The author uses the opponent's own logic against them.
2
Understand the reversal argument
The opponent claims the perfect act necessitates knowledge of the agent's knowledge. The author reverses: it only necessitates the existence of knowledge, not that the agent is knowing, because knowledge requires a substrate.
The key point: knowledge must inhere in something; it cannot stand alone.
3
Follow the second part
Then a new argument: if knowledge of accidents is inferential or necessary due to disagreement, then similarly, knowledge of the agent's attributes is inferential due to disagreement.
This mirrors the earlier disagreement argument.
4
Reflect on the counterargument
Consider why this effectively refutes the claim that knowledge of the agent is intuitive. How does the author combine both metaphysical and epistemological points?
The author combines ontology (knowledge needs a substrate) with epistemology (disagreement indicates inference).

Source Text

The Eternal Does Not Necessarily Imply Divinity
Nasafi refutes the equation of 'eternal' with 'divine' using counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'eternal' (qadim) is used in multiple contexts and does not automatically entail divinity, as shown by linguists, materialists, and even polytheists.

Core Idea
Eternal does not equal divine; context matters.

Nasafi begins by presenting the opponent's claim that evil is eternal but not God, then quickly refutes it by showing that the word 'eternal' is applied to many things without implying divinity. He draws on linguistic usage, materialist beliefs, and the polytheists' own practice of calling idols gods while acknowledging their createdness. This lesson will examine these counterexamples and ground the distinction between 'eternal' and 'divine'.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim and Nasafi's response
Start by reading the opening lines where the opponent says 'evil is eternal' and Nasafi responds by demanding proof that the eternal is the deity.
Note that the opponent does not claim evil is God, but uses the premise that evil is eternal.
2
Examine the three counterexamples
Nasafi provides three cases where 'eternal' is used without implying divinity: linguistic usage, materialists' belief in an eternal world, and polytheists calling idols gods. Identify each.
The polytheists' case is especially strong because they admit the idols are created.
3
Reflect on the Quranic evidence
Nasafi cites Quran 39:38 to show that even polytheists acknowledge God as Creator of the heavens and earth, reinforcing that they do not consider the eternal world as divine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثل السائر : الشر قديم، ولا يريدون أن الشر هو الله وأهل الدهر يعتقدون قدم كل جزء من أجزاء العالم، وما اعتقدوا ألوهيتها على أن هذا كلام يكفي منعه عن الاشتغال بإبطاله، فإنه يطالب بالدليل فيقال: لم قلت إن القديم هو الإله؟ والعقلاء بأسرهم على خلاف هذا على ما ذكرنا من إطلاق أهل اللغة لفظة القديم من غير إرادة الألوهية، واعتقاد أهل الدهر قدم العالم من غير اعتقاد الألوهية، ويسمون الأصنام آلهة وإن كانوا لا يعتقدون قدمها، فإنهم كانوا ينحتونها من الخشب والأحجار ويتخذونها من الصخر والنحاس، وهذه كلها من أجزاء العالم الذي اعتقدوا كونها مخلوقة لله تعالى على ما قال: ﴿ وَلَين سَأَلْتَهُم مَنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَ اللهُ ﴾. وما يقولون : إن القدم من أخص أوصاف الباري، فلو كانت الصفة قديمة لوجدت المشاركة في أخص الأوصاف، فتكون الصفة مثلا الله تعالى، وإذا كانت مثلا له لكانا صفتين أو موصوفين، وكلا الأمرين باطل. هذا كلام صدر عن غاية الجهل، لأنهم بنوا هذا الكلام على مقدمة مجحودة، وبعد جريان المساهلة في تسليم المقدمة غفلوا عن شريطة المماثلة ومحلها ، وبنوا علة المماثلة على أمر ممنوع، وأثبتوا حكم تلك العلة على أصل الخصوم دون أصلهم. فهذه أربع جهالات وقعوا فيها يستحيي عن الوقوع في واحدة منها الربض الخالي عن هذه الصناعة.
The common example: evil is eternal, and they do not mean that evil is God, and the materialists believe in the eternity of every part of the world, and they did not believe in their divinity, though this is speech sufficient to prevent engagement in refuting it, for it demands proof. So it is said: Why did you say that the eternal is the deity? All rational people are contrary to this, as we mentioned regarding the language scholars' use of the word 'eternal' without intending divinity, and the materialists' belief in the eternity of the world without belief in divinity, and they call idols gods even though they do not believe in their eternity, for they used to carve them from wood and stones and make them from rock and copper, and all these are parts of the world which they believed to be created by God Almighty, as He said: 'And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say Allah.' And their saying that eternity (al-qidam) is among the most specific attributes of the Creator (al-Bari), so if the attribute were eternal, there would be sharing in the most specific attributes, so the attribute would be similar to God Almighty, and if it were similar to Him, the two would be two attributes or two described entities, and both matters are invalid. This speech issued from extreme ignorance, because they built this speech on a premise that is denied, and after permitting leniency in conceding the premise, they neglected the condition of similarity and its locus, and they built the cause of similarity on a prohibited matter, and they affirmed the ruling of that cause based on a principle of the opponent without their own principle. So these are four ignorances that they fell into, such that even the feeble person devoid of this craft would be ashamed to fall into one of them.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim that 'the eternal is necessarily God'. Nasafi begins by stating the opponent's argument: they claim that evil is eternal but not God, yet they equate eternity with divinity. He then provides counterexamples: the common use of 'eternal' by linguists without implying divinity, the materialists' belief in the eternity of the world without attributing divinity to it, and the pagans who called idols gods while acknowledging they were created. He then cites the Quranic verse (39:38) to show that the polytheists admitted God created the heavens and earth. Next, he presents the opponent's syllogism: if eternity is the most specific attribute of God, and if a divine attribute were eternal, then the attribute would share in that most specific attribute, leading to either two attributes or two described entities, both false. Nasafi then identifies four ignorances in this argument: (1) building on a denied premise, (2) neglecting the condition of similarity and its locus, (3) basing the cause of similarity on a prohibited matter, and (4) applying the ruling of that cause to the opponent's own principle rather than their original principle. He concludes by stating that even a person utterly ignorant of this discipline would be ashamed to fall into any one of these.

Key Terms
القديم (al-qadim: the eternal)
الألوهية (al-uluhiyya: divinity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal always implies divine.
Correction: Eternal is a term used broadly; context determines divinity.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Nasafi's counterexamples to show that 'eternal' does not imply 'divine'?
A) Language scholars use 'qadim' without intending divinity.
B) Materialists believe the world is eternal but not divine.
C) Polytheists believe their idols are eternal.
D) The Quranic verse shows polytheists acknowledge God as Creator.

Key Takeaway
The term 'eternal' can be used in multiple contexts and does not automatically imply divinity; evidence from language, materialists, and polytheists supports this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that anything old, longstanding, or deeply rooted in your life must be from God or divinely approved? What created thing have you unknowingly given an eternal status that only Allah deserves?
?
The polytheists knew their idols were carved from wood and stone, yet they called them gods. What 'idols' in your own heart—habits, comforts, relationships—do you treat as indispensable, even while intellectually knowing they are created and temporary?
?
This lesson demands proof for the claim that 'eternal equals divine'. Where in your own beliefs about God are you relying on assumptions rather than evidence from revelation or reason? Will you examine those assumptions today?

The Fallacy of the Most Specific Attribute
Nasafi presents and critiques the opponent's syllogism about specific attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's syllogism about the most specific attributes of God and why it leads to an absurd conclusion.

Core Idea
Sharing an attribute does not entail similarity in essence.

After establishing that 'eternal' does not imply 'divine', Nasafi turns to the opponent's more refined argument: that eternity is among the most specific attributes of God, so if a divine attribute were eternal, it would share that attribute and thus be similar to God, leading to two gods. This lesson unpacks that syllogism and its flaws.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise about 'most specific attributes'
The opponent claims that eternity (al-qidam) is among the most specific attributes (akhass al-awṣāf) of God, meaning it is unique to Him.
This premise is critical for the syllogism.
2
Follow the logical steps to the conclusion
If an attribute (like knowledge or power) were eternal, it would share in that most specific attribute, making it similar to God. If similar, either there are two attributes or two described entities—both absurd.
3
Notice the vulnerability
Nasafi will later identify ignorances in this reasoning, including the assumption that sharing an attribute requires similarity in essence.
Keep in mind that the opponent's conclusion is meant to deny that any attribute of God is eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثل السائر : الشر قديم، ولا يريدون أن الشر هو الله وأهل الدهر يعتقدون قدم كل جزء من أجزاء العالم، وما اعتقدوا ألوهيتها على أن هذا كلام يكفي منعه عن الاشتغال بإبطاله، فإنه يطالب بالدليل فيقال: لم قلت إن القديم هو الإله؟ والعقلاء بأسرهم على خلاف هذا على ما ذكرنا من إطلاق أهل اللغة لفظة القديم من غير إرادة الألوهية، واعتقاد أهل الدهر قدم العالم من غير اعتقاد الألوهية، ويسمون الأصنام آلهة وإن كانوا لا يعتقدون قدمها، فإنهم كانوا ينحتونها من الخشب والأحجار ويتخذونها من الصخر والنحاس، وهذه كلها من أجزاء العالم الذي اعتقدوا كونها مخلوقة لله تعالى على ما قال: ﴿ وَلَين سَأَلْتَهُم مَنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَ اللهُ ﴾. وما يقولون : إن القدم من أخص أوصاف الباري، فلو كانت الصفة قديمة لوجدت المشاركة في أخص الأوصاف، فتكون الصفة مثلا الله تعالى، وإذا كانت مثلا له لكانا صفتين أو موصوفين، وكلا الأمرين باطل. هذا كلام صدر عن غاية الجهل، لأنهم بنوا هذا الكلام على مقدمة مجحودة، وبعد جريان المساهلة في تسليم المقدمة غفلوا عن شريطة المماثلة ومحلها ، وبنوا علة المماثلة على أمر ممنوع، وأثبتوا حكم تلك العلة على أصل الخصوم دون أصلهم. فهذه أربع جهالات وقعوا فيها يستحيي عن الوقوع في واحدة منها الربض الخالي عن هذه الصناعة.
The common example: evil is eternal, and they do not mean that evil is God, and the materialists believe in the eternity of every part of the world, and they did not believe in their divinity, though this is speech sufficient to prevent engagement in refuting it, for it demands proof. So it is said: Why did you say that the eternal is the deity? All rational people are contrary to this, as we mentioned regarding the language scholars' use of the word 'eternal' without intending divinity, and the materialists' belief in the eternity of the world without belief in divinity, and they call idols gods even though they do not believe in their eternity, for they used to carve them from wood and stones and make them from rock and copper, and all these are parts of the world which they believed to be created by God Almighty, as He said: 'And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say Allah.' And their saying that eternity (al-qidam) is among the most specific attributes of the Creator (al-Bari), so if the attribute were eternal, there would be sharing in the most specific attributes, so the attribute would be similar to God Almighty, and if it were similar to Him, the two would be two attributes or two described entities, and both matters are invalid. This speech issued from extreme ignorance, because they built this speech on a premise that is denied, and after permitting leniency in conceding the premise, they neglected the condition of similarity and its locus, and they built the cause of similarity on a prohibited matter, and they affirmed the ruling of that cause based on a principle of the opponent without their own principle. So these are four ignorances that they fell into, such that even the feeble person devoid of this craft would be ashamed to fall into one of them.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim that 'the eternal is necessarily God'. Nasafi begins by stating the opponent's argument: they claim that evil is eternal but not God, yet they equate eternity with divinity. Next, he presents the opponent's syllogism: if eternity is the most specific attribute of God, and if a divine attribute were eternal, then the attribute would share in that most specific attribute, leading to either two attributes or two described entities, both false. Nasafi then identifies four ignorances in this argument: (1) building on a denied premise, (2) neglecting the condition of similarity and its locus, (3) basing the cause of similarity on a prohibited matter, and (4) applying the ruling of that cause to the opponent's own principle rather than their original principle. He concludes by stating that even a person utterly ignorant of this discipline would be ashamed to fall into any one of these.

Key Terms
أخص الأوصاف (akhass al-awṣāf: most specific attributes)
المماثلة (al-mumathala: similarity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing any attribute with God makes one similar to Him in essence.
Correction: Sharing an attribute does not entail essential similarity; attributes are not the essence.

Checkpoint
What does the opponent conclude if an attribute of God were eternal?
A) The attribute would be created.
B) The attribute would be similar to God, leading to an absurdity.
C) The attribute would be separate from God.
D) The attribute would be a different god.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's syllogism attempts to show that no attribute of God can be eternal by invoking the concept of 'most specific attributes', but the reasoning contains flaws that Nasafi will expose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute a specific quality to someone, do you ever assume they are just like you in that respect? How has this led you to misunderstand or misjudge them? Commit to recognizing that shared attributes do not mean identical essence.
?
The opponent's error was to assume that sharing the attribute of eternity implies similarity in essence. In your own life, where have you mistakenly thought that because you share a trait with another, you are essentially the same? What would it cost you to accept that difference at the essence level?
?
This passage warns against conflating qualitative similarity with essential identity. How comfortable are you with the fact that God's attributes are unlike anything we know? Is there an area where you secretly wish God were more like you? Confess it and resolve to submit to His transcendence.
