The Four Ignorances of the Opponent
Nasafi lists and explains four ignorances in the opponent's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical flaws (ignorances) that Nasafi identifies in the opponent's reasoning.

Core Idea
The opponent's argument is built on four ignorances.

Nasafi concludes his refutation by exposing four distinct ignorances (jahalat) in the opponent's argument. These range from building on a denied premise to misapplying the condition of similarity and basing the ruling on the wrong principle. This lesson will enumerate and explain each ignorance, showing why even a novice in theology would be embarrassed to commit such errors.

Guided Path
1
First ignorance: Building on a denied premise
The opponent's entire argument rests on the premise that eternity is among the most specific attributes of God, but this premise is not accepted by all. Nasafi calls it 'majhuda' (denied).
2
Second ignorance: Neglecting the condition of similarity
Even if we concede the premise, the opponent ignores the conditions (sharāʾiṭ) and locus (maḥall) of similarity. Not every shared attribute creates similarity.
3
Third ignorance: Building the cause of similarity on a prohibited matter
The opponent's reasoning about what causes similarity (ʿilla al-mumathala) is based on something prohibited (mamnūʿ), meaning it is not valid.
4
Fourth ignorance: Applying the ruling to the opponent's principle, not their own
The opponent applies the ruling of similarity to a principle that belongs to their adversary (khuṣūm) rather than to their own principle, making the argument self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثل السائر : الشر قديم، ولا يريدون أن الشر هو الله وأهل الدهر يعتقدون قدم كل جزء من أجزاء العالم، وما اعتقدوا ألوهيتها على أن هذا كلام يكفي منعه عن الاشتغال بإبطاله، فإنه يطالب بالدليل فيقال: لم قلت إن القديم هو الإله؟ والعقلاء بأسرهم على خلاف هذا على ما ذكرنا من إطلاق أهل اللغة لفظة القديم من غير إرادة الألوهية، واعتقاد أهل الدهر قدم العالم من غير اعتقاد الألوهية، ويسمون الأصنام آلهة وإن كانوا لا يعتقدون قدمها، فإنهم كانوا ينحتونها من الخشب والأحجار ويتخذونها من الصخر والنحاس، وهذه كلها من أجزاء العالم الذي اعتقدوا كونها مخلوقة لله تعالى على ما قال: ﴿ وَلَين سَأَلْتَهُم مَنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَ اللهُ ﴾. وما يقولون : إن القدم من أخص أوصاف الباري، فلو كانت الصفة قديمة لوجدت المشاركة في أخص الأوصاف، فتكون الصفة مثلا الله تعالى، وإذا كانت مثلا له لكانا صفتين أو موصوفين، وكلا الأمرين باطل. هذا كلام صدر عن غاية الجهل، لأنهم بنوا هذا الكلام على مقدمة مجحودة، وبعد جريان المساهلة في تسليم المقدمة غفلوا عن شريطة المماثلة ومحلها ، وبنوا علة المماثلة على أمر ممنوع، وأثبتوا حكم تلك العلة على أصل الخصوم دون أصلهم. فهذه أربع جهالات وقعوا فيها يستحيي عن الوقوع في واحدة منها الربض الخالي عن هذه الصناعة.

?

Is there an area in your life where you are using worldly standards to judge divine matters, or justifying your actions based on the values of those you disagree with? Refutation of the Third Ignorance on the Cause of Similarity
Nasafi objects to making commonality in the most specific attribute the cause of similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why using a disputed causal principle to refute an opponent is logically invalid in theological argumentation.

Core Idea
You cannot defeat an opponent with a premise they reject.

In this passage, Nasafi continues his critique of those who hold to the third ignorance regarding the cause of similarity. They claimed that the cause of similarity is sharing in the most specific attribute. Nasafi points out that this principle itself is disputed; thus, using it to refute those who do not accept it is an invalid argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for familiarity, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: ʿilla, mumāthala, akhaṣṣ al-awṣāf.
The passage is concise, so note every phrase.
2
Identify the disputed causal principle
The opponents assert that the cause (ʿilla) of similarity is 'sharing in the most specific attribute' (al-istirāk fī akhaṣṣ al-awṣāf). Recognize that this is their own school's position.
Nasafi does not explain the principle here; he only critiques its application.
3
Understand Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi states that this principle is not accepted by their opponents, and he has already shown its corruption. Therefore, using it to refute those who reject it is invalid—it's an argument that begs the question.
Consider how the validity of a refutation depends on shared premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجهالة الثالثة : فهي أنهم جعلوا علة المماثلة الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف، وهذا مذهبهم ويخالفهم خصومهم في ذلك على ما قررنا وبينا فساد ذلك، فكان التعلق به لإبطال مذهب من لا يساعدهم على ذلك باطلا.
As for the third ignorance: It is that they made the cause (ʿilla) of similarity the sharing in the most specific attribute (akhaṣṣ al-awṣāf). This is their school, and their opponents disagree with them on that, as we have established and shown the corruption of that. So clinging to it to invalidate the school of those who do not support them on that is invalid.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the third ignorance. Nasafi states that the opponents (the ones being refuted) have made the cause (ʿilla) of similarity as the sharing in the most specific attribute (al-istirāk fī akhaṣṣ al-awṣāf). This is their own doctrinal position, but their opponents (e.g., the Ashʿaris) disagree with them on that causal principle. Nasafi has already explained and demonstrated the corruption of that principle in previous discussions. Therefore, clinging to this disputed causal principle to refute the school of those who do not accept it is invalid. In other words, you cannot use a premise that your opponent rejects to defeat them.

Key Terms
علة
المماثلة
أخص الأوصاف
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that citing one's own school's principle suffices to refute another school.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the principle itself must be accepted by the opponent; otherwise, the argument is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the problem with the third ignorance according to Nasafi?
A) The opponents used a cause that is not agreed upon.
B) The opponents denied the existence of attributes.
C) The opponents used an analogy from the well.
D) The opponents claimed that similarity is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Using a causal principle that your opponent rejects cannot serve as a valid basis for refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you ever tried to argue for that belief without first checking whether the other person shares your most basic assumptions? What did that experience teach you about the effectiveness of your arguments?
?
In your own life, when you defend a truth, are you willing to set aside a premise that is not shared by your listener? Or do you insist on it because it seems obvious to you? How might that insistence actually hinder the truth you aim to convey?
?
If you were in a debate with someone who rejects a principle you believe is foundational, what would it cost you to find common ground instead of doubling down? Are you ready to take that step in your next difficult conversation?

Refutation of the Fourth Ignorance: Inconsistent Application of the Causal Principle
Nasafi refutes the fourth ignorance by showing the inconsistency in the opponents' causal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes the inconsistency of applying a specific causal principle to claim similarity in all attributes.

Core Idea
A specific cause gives a specific ruling, not a universal one.

This passage tackles a key sectarian disagreement about the causal principle of similarity. The opponents claim to use a specific cause but then derive a general ruling, which actually follows the Ash'ari school. Nasafi uses the example of two whitenesses to clarify the logical error. Understanding this refutation is crucial for grasping the Maturidi position on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the compressed style and the use of an example.
2
Identify the two causal principles
The passage contrasts two views: one holds that the cause of similarity is sharing in the most specific attribute; the other (Ash'ari) holds it is sharing in all attributes. Highlight these in the text.
The opponents claim to follow the first but end up with the second.
3
Follow the whiteness example
Work through the example of two whitenesses. Note what similarity the specific cause entails (only in whiteness) vs. what a general cause would entail (in all attributes).
This example is then applied to the divine essence and attributes.
4
Apply to divine attributes
See how the same logic applies to the sharing of qidam between essence and attribute. The specific cause (qidam) only gives equality in qidam, not in being gods or attributes.
The opponents' error is demanding more than the cause allows.
5
Reflect on the inconsistency
Nasafi concludes that the opponents have actually adopted the Ash'ari principle while claiming to use their own. This invalidates their argument.
This is a classic argumentative move: showing that the opposition's position collapses into another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجهالة الرابعة فهي إثبات حكم علة المماثلة على مذهب خصومهم دون مذهبهم فهي أن علة المماثلة عندهم الاشتراك في وصف واحد وهو أخص الأوصاف والأشعرية يقولون : إن علة المماثلة الاشتراك في جميع الأوصاف. فكانت العلة عندهم عامة فكان الحكم عاما، فلما اشترك البياضان في جميع الموصوفات كانت المماثلة بينهما ثابتة في جميع الأوصاف، فكانا مثلين من جميع الوجوه، وإذا كان كذلك وكانت علة المماثلة عندهم هو الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف كان الحكم وهو المماثلة بيانا فى أخص الأوصاف لا في الجميع، فإن البياضين كانت المماثلة الثانية بينهما في كونهما بياضين لثبوت الاشتراك بينهما في وصف البياض لا غير، فأما استواء البياضين في كونهما لونين عرضين والعرضية واللونية توجب اقتصار الحكم على قدر العلة، وإذا عرف هذا ثبت أن اشتراك الذات والصفة في صفة القدم يوجب استوائهما في القدم لا غير، والخصوم لا يأبون هذا، فأما استوائهما في كونهما إلهين أو موصوفين أو صفتين فليس بحكم لاستوائهما في القدم، فإذا أثبتوا الاستواء في هذه الأوصاف لوجود الاشتراك في أخص أوصاف النفس، فقد أثبتوا المماثلة في جميع الوجوه كما هو مذهب الأشعرية بوجود الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف على ما هو مذهبهم دون مذهب الأشعرية
As for the fourth ignorance: it is the establishment of the ruling of the cause of similarity according to the school of their opponents, not according to their own school. That is, the cause of similarity according to them is sharing in one attribute, which is the most specific attribute. But the Ashʿaris say that the cause of similarity is sharing in all attributes. So the cause according to them is general, so the ruling is general. Thus, when two whitenesses share in all attributes, similarity between them is established in all attributes, so they are similar in all respects. And if it is so, and the cause of similarity according to them is sharing in the most specific attribute, then the ruling—which is similarity—is manifest in the most specific attribute, not in all. For two whitenesses: the secondary similarity between them is in their being whitenesses, due to the established sharing between them in the attribute of whiteness alone, not otherwise. As for the equality of the two whitenesses in being colors, accidents, and the accidents-ness and color-ness, this requires restricting the ruling to the extent of the cause. And when this is known, it is established that the sharing of the Essence and the attribute in the attribute of qidam necessitates their equality in qidam alone, and the opponents do not deny this. As for their equality in being gods, or described, or attributes, it is not a ruling due to their equality in qidam. So if they establish equality in these attributes due to the existence of sharing in the most specific attribute of the self (or essence), then they have established similarity in all respects, as is the school of the Ashʿaris, by the existence of sharing in the most specific attribute according to their school, not the school of the Ashʿaris.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the fourth ignorance. Nasafi explains that the opponents erroneously apply the ruling of the cause (ʿilla) of similarity according to the school of their opponents (the Ashʿaris) rather than their own. According to the opponents (likely the Maturidis or others being refuted?), the cause of similarity is sharing in one attribute, the most specific. But the Ashʿaris hold that the cause is sharing in all attributes. So the Ashʿari cause is general, leading to a general ruling of similarity in all respects. Nasafi uses an example: two whitenesses. If the cause is sharing in all attributes, then they are similar in all attributes. But if the cause is only sharing in the most specific attribute (whiteness), then similarity is only in that attribute, not in being colors, accidents, etc. The ruling is restricted to the extent of the cause. Applying this to God: the Essence and the attribute share in qidam, so equality in qidam alone is entailed. The opponents (Ashʿaris?) do not deny that. However, if they claim equality in being gods or being described, that would require a broader cause. If they insist that sharing in the most specific attribute (qidam) yields equality in all attributes, they are actually adopting the Ashʿari principle (sharing in all attributes) while claiming to use their own principle—an inconsistency. Thus, their argument is invalid.

Key Terms

حكم
الاشتراك
أخص الأوصاف
الأشعرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A specific cause (like sharing in one attribute) can justify a general ruling (similarity in all attributes).
Correction: The ruling is restricted to the extent of the cause. A specific cause only gives a specific ruling.

Checkpoint
What inconsistency does Nasafi expose in the fourth ignorance?
A) The opponents claim similarity in all attributes while using a cause that only gives similarity in the most specific attribute.
B) The opponents deny the existence of attributes.
C) The opponents use the same cause as the Ashʿaris.
D) The opponents' analysis of whiteness is incorrect.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that applying a specific causal principle to claim universal similarity is logically inconsistent and effectively adopts the Ash'ari position.

How does this inconsistency manifest in your own reasoning about Allah's attributes? What step will you take today to ensure your arguments about the divine are logically consistent?
?
When you think about the relationship between Allah's essence and His attributes, do you inadvertently impose human categories of similarity that go beyond what the texts warrant? This passage warns against overextending causal reasoning. In what area of your faith do you need to re-examine your assumptions to avoid such inconsistency?
?
The opponents claimed to use a specific cause but actually adopted the general one. In your own life, do you claim to follow the Quran and Sunnah while actually adopting principles from other sources (culture, personal preference) that are inconsistent with them? How can you realign your reasoning to be consistent with the teachings of Ahl al-Sunna?

Refutation of the Third Ignorance: Invalid Use of Own Premise
Nasafi declares the third ignorance invalid due to reliance on own contested premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's third argument—that similarity is caused by sharing in the most specific attribute—fails when used against opponents who reject that premise.

Core Idea
You cannot refute an opponent using a premise they do not accept.

Imam Nasafi now turns to the third of four 'ignorances' he attributes to the Mu'tazila. This argument revolves around the Mu'tazila's own definition of the cause of similarity. The problem? They are trying to use their own contested principle against the Ash'aris, who explicitly reject it. Nasafi exposes this as a logically invalid move, showing that such an argument carries no weight in a debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila's claim
Read the passage and note what the Mu'tazila assert as the cause of similarity (ʿillat al-mumāthala).
They say it is sharing in the most specific attributes (akhaṣṣ al-ṣifāt).
2
Recognize the problem
Understand that this is their own school's position, not accepted by their opponents (the Ashʿarīs).
The opponents disagree, as Nasafi has already shown the corruption of this view.
3
See the logical flaw
Realize that using a premise that your opponent rejects to refute them is invalid—it begs the question.
Nasafi calls this 'holding on to it' to invalidate the school of someone who does not help them in that.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this is a key point in theological debate: arguments must be built on shared ground.
This is a basic principle of disputation (jadal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجهالة الثالثة : فهي أنهم جعلوا علة المماثلة الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف، وهذا مذهبهم ويخالفهم خصومهم في ذلك على ما قررنا وبينا فساد ذلك، فكان التعلق به لإبطال مذهب من لا يساعدهم على ذلك باطلا. وأما الجهالة الرابعة فهي إثبات حكم علة المماثلة على مذهب خصومهم دون مذهبهم فهي أن علة المماثلة عندهم الاشتراك في وصف واحد وهو أخص الأوصاف والأشعرية يقولون : إن علة المماثلة الاشتراك في جميع الأوصاف. فكانت العلة عندهم عامة فكان الحكم عاما، فلما اشترك البياضان في جميع الموصوفات كانت المماثلة بينهما ثابتة في جميع الأوصاف، فكانا مثلين من جميع الوجوه، وإذا كان كذلك وكانت علة المماثلة عندهم هو الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف كان الحكم وهو المماثلة بيانا فى أخص الأوصاف لا في الجميع، فإن البياضين كانت المماثلة الثانية بينهما في كونهما بياضين لثبوت الاشتراك بينهما في وصف البياض لا غير، فأما استواء البياضين في كونهما لونين عرضين والعرضية واللونية توجب اقتصار الحكم على قدر العلة، وإذا عرف هذا ثبت أن اشتراك الذات والصفة في صفة القدم يوجب استوائهما في القدم لا غير، والخصوم لا يأبون هذا، فأما استوائهما في كونهما إلهين أو موصوفين أو صفتين فليس بحكم لاستوائهما في القدم، فإذا أثبتوا الاستواء في هذه الأوصاف لوجود الاشتراك في أخص أوصاف النفس، فقد أثبتوا المماثلة في جميع الوجوه كما هو مذهب الأشعرية بوجود الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف على ما هو مذهبهم دون مذهب الأشعرية، فكان الخطأ فيه من وجهين أحدهما - إثبات الحكم لعلة زيادة على قدر العلة - وهو محال، والثاني طريق العموم والشمول كما هو حكم علة الخصوم، وفساد هذا ظاهر، لما أن كل ذلك خارج عن قضية العقول، وجهل بخاصة الحد والمحدود والعلة والمعلول، وهذا ليعلم أن من دأب المعتزلة التهويل بلا تحصيل والتعويل على التخييل، وكل كلام لهم لو عرض على قوانين الأصول، ومقتضيات العقول لظهر أنه (بريق السراب بقيمة)، والله الموفق.
As for the third ignorance: it is that they made the cause of similarity (ʿillat al-mumāthala) the sharing in the most specific attributes (akhaṣṣ al-ṣifāt), and this is their school, while their opponents disagree with them in that, as we have established and shown the corruption of that, so holding on to it to invalidate the school of one who does not help them in that is invalid. As for the fourth ignorance: it is establishing the ruling of the cause of similarity according to the school of their opponents, not their own school. That is, the cause of similarity according to them is sharing in one attribute, which is the most specific attribute, while the Ashʿarīs say: the cause of similarity is sharing in all attributes. So the cause according to them is general, so the ruling is general. Since whitenesses share in all described things, similarity between them is established in all attributes, so they become two similars in all respects. If that is so, and the cause of similarity according to them is sharing in the most specific attribute, then the ruling—which is similarity—is specific to the most specific attributes, not to all. For two whitenesses, similarity between them is in being whitenesses, due to the established sharing between them in the attribute of whiteness only. As for the equality of the two whitenesses in being colors, accidents, etc., that is not the ruling. Because generality of the cause and specificity of attributes necessitates restricting the ruling to the extent of the cause. Once this is known, it is established that the sharing of essence and attribute in the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam) necessitates their equality in pre-eternity only, and the opponents do not deny this. As for their equality in being gods, or described, or attributes, that is not a ruling due to their equality in pre-eternity. If they establish equality in these attributes due to the existence of sharing in the most specific attribute of the self, then they have established similarity in all respects, as is the Ashʿarī school, by the existence of sharing in the most specific attribute according to their school, not the Ashʿarī school. So the error in it is from two aspects: first, establishing a ruling for a cause beyond the extent of the cause, and that is impossible; second, the method of generality and inclusiveness as is the ruling of the opponent's cause. The corruption of this is clear, because all that is beyond the dictates of intellects, and ignorance of the specificity of the definition and the defined, the cause and the effect. This is so that it be known that the habit of the Muʿtazila is bluster without verification and reliance on deception. All their speech, if presented to the principles of fundamental principles (uṣūl) and the requirements of intellects, would appear to be 'the gleam of a mirage of worth'. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Imam Nasafi of two arguments (called 'ignorances') of the Mu'tazila regarding the concept of similarity (mumāthala) between the divine essence and attributes. The Imam first explains the third ignorance: the Mu'tazila claim that the cause of similarity is sharing in the most specific attribute(s). Nasafi argues that this is their own position, not accepted by their opponents (the Ashʿarīs), so using it to refute those who reject it is invalid. He then addresses the fourth ignorance: the Mu'tazila apply the cause of similarity according to the opponents' school (Ashʿarī) but only partially. The Ashʿarīs hold that similarity requires sharing in all attributes, whereas the Mu'tazila hold it requires only one attribute. Nasafi uses the example of two white objects: under the Ashʿarī view, they are similar in all respects; under the Mu'tazila view, they are similar only in whiteness. The author then applies this to the divine: the sharing of essence and attribute in pre-eternity (qidam) means they are equal only in pre-eternity, not in all respects (like being gods). If the Mu'tazila claim equality in all respects based on one specific attribute, they are inadvertently adopting the Ashʿarī position. Nasafi highlights two errors: (1) extending the ruling beyond the cause, and (2) using the opponent's method of generality/universality. He concludes that the Mu'tazila's arguments are mere bluster and deception, contrary to sound intellect and principles.

Key Terms
علة المماثلة
أخص الأوصاف
جهالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can use one's own theological premise in a debate even if the opponent rejects that premise.
Correction: Nasafi shows that using an uncontested premise against an opponent who denies it is invalid; the argument fails because it lacks shared ground.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Nasafi consider the Mu'tazila's third ignorance invalid?
A) Because the Mu'tazila deny the Ashʿarī school altogether.
B) Because the Mu'tazila use their own contested premise against opponents who reject it.
C) Because the Mu'tazila claim similarity in all attributes.
D) Because the Mu'tazila admit that their doctrine is corrupt.

Key Takeaway
The third ignorance is invalid because it builds an argument on a premise that the opponent does not accept—a logical dead end in theological debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you argued about a religious matter using a premise that the other person fully rejected. How did that affect the discussion, and what does this lesson teach you about the foundation of fruitful debate? Will you commit to checking shared premises before arguing?
?
When you defend a belief about Allah’s attributes, are you relying on assumptions that come from your own school of thought? If your opponent does not share those assumptions, what should you do instead of simply asserting your premise? This lesson exposes a common fallacy—are you willing to change your debating habits?
?
The Mu'tazila used a premise that only they accepted. In your own spiritual life, have you ever built an argument or conviction on a foundation that others (or even you) might later discover is not universally true? How can you ensure your core beliefs about Allah are built on the consensus of the Salaf rather than sectarian assumptions?

Refutation of the Fourth Ignorance: Two Errors in Applying the Cause
Nasafi critiques the Mu'tazila's fourth argument, highlighting two logical errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two errors in the Mu'tazila's fourth argument: extending the ruling beyond the cause and inadvertently adopting the Ashʿarī method of generality.

Core Idea
Similarity in one attribute does not imply similarity in all attributes.

The fourth ignorance is more elaborate. Here the Mu'tazila attempt to apply the cause of similarity according to the Ashʿarī school's definition—sharing in all attributes—but only partially. Nasafi dissects this with a concrete example of two white objects and then applies it to the divine attributes of essence and pre-eternity. He exposes two fundamental errors: exceeding the cause and using the opponent's method of generality. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila's fourth argument
Read the passage and note that the Mu'tazila are trying to use the Ashʿarī definition of cause (sharing in all attributes) but only partially applying it.
They claim similarity in the most specific attribute, not in all.
2
Follow the example of two whitenesses
Nasafi gives an example: two white objects are similar only in whiteness, not in being colors, accidents, etc. This illustrates the difference between partial and total similarity.
The cause (sharing in whiteness) yields similarity only in whiteness.
3
Apply to the divine
The sharing of essence and attribute in pre-eternity (qidam) means they are similar only in pre-eternity, not in being gods or attributes.
The Mu'tazila's mistake is to claim equality in all respects based on one attribute.
4
Identify the two errors
Nasafi lists two errors: (1) establishing a ruling beyond the extent of the cause, and (2) using the opponent's method of generality while rejecting their position.
This shows the argument is contradictory and intellectually unsound.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila's arguments are mere bluster and deception, contrary to sound intellect and principles.
The final phrase 'gleam of a mirage' emphasizes their illusory nature.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Imam Nasafi of two arguments (called 'ignorances') of the Mu'tazila regarding the concept of similarity (mumāthala) between the divine essence and attributes. The Imam first explains the third ignorance: the Mu'tazila claim that the cause of similarity is sharing in the most specific attribute(s). Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share one attribute, they are similar in all attributes.

Why does Imam Nasafi consider the Mu'tazila's fourth ignorance to contain two errors?
A) They apply a cause to a ruling greater than itself and use the opponents' method of generality.

D) They reject the Ashʿarī school completely.

?

Refuting Abu Hashim's Definition of Al-Ghayrayn
Refutation of Abu Hashim's definition of al-ghayrayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hashim al-Mu'tazili's definition of al-ghayrayn is criticized as linguistically flawed and inconsistent with his own doctrine of states (ahwal).

Core Idea
A flawed definition of 'otherness' can undermine an entire theological system.

This passage is a vigorous refutation of a Mu'tazilite definition of al-ghayrayn. Abu Hashim defined two things as 'other' if neither is a totality that contains the other. The author argues that this definition fails because it relies on the term 'mentioned' (which includes non-existents) and contradicts Abu Hashim's own doctrine of states. Prepare to see how precise definitions in kalam can have far-reaching consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and examples
Read Abu Hashim's definition of al-ghayrayn as 'two mentioned things where neither is a totality that includes the other.' Note his examples: one from ten, and man with his limbs.
Pay attention to the idea of 'totality' (jumla).
2
Understand the first critique
The author points out that 'mentioned' applies to both existent and nonexistent, but 'other' is only used for existents by linguistic convention, so the definition is too broad.
3
See the second critique from states
According to Abu Hashim's own theory, states (ahwal) are 'mentioned' but are not other than each other, yet his definition would make them others—a contradiction.
This shows the definition fails on his own principles.
4
Reflect on the accusation
The author accuses Abu Hashim of feigning ignorance and intellectual dishonesty, especially regarding the status of states as 'mentioned' or not.
Consider how this accusation ties to the overall refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو هاشم من المعتزلة أن الغيرين مذكوران لا يكون أحدهما جملة يدخل تحتها الآخر، واحترز بهذا عن الواحد من العشرة، والإنسان مع أعضائه فكل عضو من ( الإنسان ليس غير الإنسان وإن كان لا يقال له هو، وكذا الواحد من العشرة ولكن كان الإنسان جملة يدخل تحتها كل عضو معين وكذا العشرة جملة دخل تحتها الأحاد، فكان محلاً واحدًا داخلا تحت العشرة . وهذا التحديد فاسد، لأن لفظة المذكور كما تتناول الموجود تتناول المعدوم وإطلاق اسم الغير على المعدوم فاسد (لأن لفظه) (۱) يأباه أهل اللغة، بل هو اسم يتناول أحد الموجودين باعتبار الآخر ثم هو على أصله غير مطرد فإن الأحوال عنده مذكورات وليست أحديها جملة تدخل تحتها الأخرى، ومع ذلك ليست بمتغايرات وتجاهله أن الأحوال ليست بمذكورة (۲) فاسد لأنه ذكرها حيث (۳) أثبتها ليصير لذات بها مستحقا اسم العالم والقادر ، ثم اشتغال أبي هاشم بإفساد القول: أن الصفة لا هذا ضرورة، خروجا عن المعقول مع زعمه أن الأحوال لا هي الذات ولا غيره، ومساواتنا في ذلك، ثم اشتغاله بالتجاهل أنها ليست بمذكورة ولا غير مذكورة، ولا معلومة ولا لا معلومة ولا موجودة ولا معدومة. غاية الوقاحة وقلة المبالاة من التخبط في دين الله تعالى.
And Abu Hashim from the Mu'tazila claimed that al-ghayrayn are two mentioned things (madhkuran) such that neither is a totality that includes the other. He guarded by this against the one from ten, and man with his limbs: for every limb of man is not other than man even if it is not said it is he, and likewise the one from ten. But man is a totality that includes every specific limb, and likewise ten is a totality that includes the ones, so that is a single locus included under ten. And this definition is corrupt, because the word 'mentioned' (al-madhkur) applies to the existent as it applies to the nonexistent, and applying the name 'other' to the nonexistent is corrupt, for the people of language reject it. Rather, it is a name that applies to one of the two existents in consideration of the other. Then it is not consistent according to his own principle, for the states (al-ahwal) according to him are mentioned things, and none of them is a totality that includes another, yet they are not mutually others. And his ignorance that the states are not mentioned is corrupt, because he mentions them when he affirms them for the essence to become deserving of the name 'knower' and 'powerful' by them. Then Abu Hashim's occupation with corrupting the statement that the attribute is not He is an unnecessary departure from reason, along with his claim that the states are neither the essence nor other than it, and our equality in that. Then his occupation with feigning ignorance that they are neither mentioned nor unmentioned, neither known nor unknown, neither existent nor nonexistent. This is the utmost shamelessness and lack of concern in stumbling about the religion of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu Hashim al-Mu'tazili's definition of al-ghayrayn. Abu Hashim defines them as two 'mentioned' (madhkuran) things such that neither is a totality (jumla) that includes the other. He gives examples: the one from ten is not other than ten because ten is a totality including the ones; the hand is not other than man because man is a totality including the hand. The author then refutes this definition. First, the term 'mentioned' applies to both existent and nonexistent, but 'other' is only used for existents by linguistic convention. Second, Abu Hashim's own doctrine of states (ahwal) undermines his definition: states are 'mentioned' in his system but are not other than each other, nor is one a totality including the other, yet he claims they are not mutually others. The author accuses Abu Hashim of inconsistency and intellectual dishonesty—he first posits states to account for attributes, then pretends they are not 'mentioned' to avoid the implication that they are other than the essence. The author ends with a strong condemnation of such reasoning.

Key Terms
المذكور (al-madhkur)
الأحوال (al-ahwal)
الجملة (al-jumla)
الغير (al-ghayr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'mentioned' applies only to existent things.
Correction: Abu Hashim's definition relies on 'mentioned' which also applies to non-existents, making it too broad.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say Abu Hashim's definition of al-ghayrayn is 'not consistent according to his own principle'?
A) Because it applies to non-existents.
B) Because states (ahwal) are mentioned but not others, contradicting his own doctrine.
C) Because it includes the example of the one from ten.
D) Because the definition is too narrow.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's definition of al-ghayrayn is rejected because it is linguistically imprecise and contradicts his own theory of states, revealing an inconsistency in Mu'tazilite kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a position in religious matters, are you willing to admit when it leads to inconsistency? Or do you, like Abu Hashim, feign ignorance to avoid the implication? Ask yourself: where in your own understanding of Islam do you resist correction because it would upend something you've built?
?
The author accuses Abu Hashim of 'utmost shamelessness and lack of concern' in stumbling about religion. Have you ever dismissed a valid critique of your beliefs with a wave of the hand rather than engaging it honestly? What would it cost you today to examine one such area with complete intellectual honesty?
?
Abu Hashim's definition of 'otherness' seemed precise but collapsed under scrutiny. Are there definitions or labels you use in your faith that you have never tested against the primary sources? Commit to checking one such term against the Quran and Sunnah this week.

Refuting the Definition of al-Ghayrayn as Differing in Description
The author refutes the definition of al-ghayrayn as those differing in description, using two counterexamples and extending to a related view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili definition of al-ghayrayn as 'those differing in description' is invalid, based on counterexamples from the Maturidi critique.

Core Idea
Difference in description neither guarantees nor prevents otherness.

The Mu'tazila attempted to define otherness (al-ghayriyya) as difference in description. The author provides two powerful counterexamples that show this definition fails both within their own system and universally. This lesson examines these examples and their implications for the nature of otherness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili definition
Read the opening statement of the passage: some Mu'tazila defined al-ghayrayn as those differing in description. Grasp this definition clearly before proceeding.
This is presented as a claim to be refuted.
2
Examine the first counterexample
Consider the example of two blacknesses (sawdan) in one locus. According to Mu'tazili own doctrine, two blacknesses can coexist in the same place at the same time. They are two others (ghiran) but do not differ in description (both are blackness). This disproves the definition from within their own system.
Note that this example targets the necessity condition: difference in description is not necessary for otherness.
3
Examine the second counterexample
Consider the examples of 'one from ten' and 'hand from human'. The one and ten differ in description (one is singular, ten is plural), but they are not others because the one is part of the ten. Similarly, hand and human differ but the hand is part of the human. This shows difference in description is not sufficient for otherness.
This targets the sufficiency condition.
4
See the extension to a related view
The author then refutes the view that 'whatever is not the thing is other than it' using the same examples: the one is not the ten but is not other than it. This shows that negation alone does not imply otherness.
This extends the refutation to a broader principle.
5
Note the exception of Ja'far ibn Harb
The passage ends by mentioning Ja'far ibn Harb, who held that the one from ten is other than the ten. This indicates a minority view that still does not salvage the definition.
The author dismisses this as an exception without further argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض المعتزلة : الغيران هما المختلفان في الوصف. وهذا باطل على أصلهم فإن من مذهبهم أن قيام سوداين في وقت واحد في محل واحد) (٤) جائز وهما غيران ولا يختلفان بوصف ما ثم هو باطل عند الجميع بالواحد من العشرة واليد من الآدمي فإن الواحد مع العشرة يختلفان في الوصف، وكذا اليد مع الآدمي ) (٥) (ولا تغاير وبهذا يبطل قول من يقول : إن ما ليس بالشيء فهو غيره فإن الواحد ليس بالعشرة وليس (٢٦) بغيرها وكذا اليد من الآدمي )) فإن اتبع واحد منهم رأي جعفر بن حرب (۸) وزعم أن الواحد من العشرة غير العشرة واليد من الآدمي
And some Mu'tazila said: The two others (al-ghiran) are those differing in description (al-mukhtalifan fi al-wasf). And this is invalid according to their principle, for part of their doctrine is that the existence of two blacknesses at one time in one locus is permissible, and they are two others and do not differ in any description. Then it is invalid according to all by the one from ten and the hand from the human: for the one and ten differ in description, and likewise the hand and the human, and there is no otherness. And by this is invalidated the statement of whoever says: Whatever is not the thing is other than it, for the one is not the ten and is not other than it, and likewise the hand from the human. Then if one of them follows the view of Ja'far ibn Harb (8) and claims that the one from ten is other than the ten and the hand from the human is other than the human...
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage refutes another Mu'tazili definition: al-ghayrayn are those that differ in description (al-mukhtalifan fi al-wasf). The author provides two counterexamples. First, according to the Mu'tazili school itself, two blacknesses (sawdan) can exist simultaneously in the same locus; they are two others (ghiran) yet do not differ in description since both are blackness. This invalidates the definition from within their own system. Second, for all groups (and the Mu'tazila included) the example of one from ten and hand from human: the one and ten differ in description (one is single, ten is plural), but they are not others (the one is part of the ten). Similarly, the hand and the human differ but are not others. Thus difference in description is not sufficient for otherness. The author then extends this to refute the view that 'whatever is not the thing is other than it' (ma laysa bi-l-shay' fahuwa ghayruhu), because the one is not the ten but is not other than it. Finally, he mentions Ja'far ibn Harb who held that the one is other than the ten, implying that such a view is an exception and still incorrect.

Key Terms
الغيران (al-ghiran)
المختلفان في الوصف (al-mukhtalifan fi al-wasf)
السوداين (al-sawdan)
الواحد من العشرة (al-wahid min al-ashara)
اليد من الآدمي (al-yad min al-adami)
تغاير (taghayyur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Difference in description is sufficient to establish otherness between two things.
Correction: Two things can differ in description yet not be 'others' (e.g., part and whole). Conversely, two things can be others without differing in description (e.g., two blacknesses).

Checkpoint
Which two examples does the author use to refute the definition that al-ghayrayn are those differing in description?
A) Two blacknesses in one locus and the one from ten.
B) Father and son, and high and low.
C) Essence and attribute, and the one from ten.
D) Man and his limbs, and two blacknesses.

Key Takeaway
Difference in description is neither necessary nor sufficient for two things to be considered 'other' (ghayr).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone as 'other' simply because they differ from you in description—appearance, opinion, or status—are you missing the reality that they may be intrinsically part of your own human fabric? What would it take for you to see them as a hand to your body rather than a stranger?
?
The author refutes the Mu'tazili definition by showing that two blacknesses can be distinct yet identical in description. In your own soul, are there impulses or sins that appear identical but are actually two distinct failings? Are you conflating them, and how does that conflation prevent you from repenting properly?
?
Would you rather cling to a neat definition that fails reality, like the Mu'tazila, or accept a messier truth that preserves your relationship with Allah? Is there a doctrine you hold about Allah's attributes that actually pushes Him away from you because you demand a clear-cut 'otherness'? Let this lesson strip away that delusion.

The Mu'tazili Claim and Its Refutation
Nasafi introduces the opponent's argument and declares its weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili claim of similarity between God's knowledge and human knowledge, and why citing their foremost scholar's weak argument undermines the entire school.

Core Idea
Two similars cannot be one eternal and one created — this logical principle destroys the Mu'tazili analogy.

Nasafi sets the stage by quoting the Mu'tazili argument in their own words. He shows that their reasoning leads to a logical impossibility. Then, rather than attacking a strawman, he cites their most brilliant scholar — yet even his argument is flimsy. This prepares the reader to see the weakness of the entire Mu'tazili position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice that Nasafi is quoting his opponent's own words.
2
Identify the logical argument
Find the Mu'tazili claim that God's knowledge is similar to human knowledge. Then identify the impossibility derived from the principle of similars: two similar things cannot be one eternal and one created.
The key phrase is 'لزمهم أن يكون المعنى...'.
3
Understand the rhetorical strategy
Notice how Nasafi praises the opponent's intelligence and status before dismissing his argument. Ask yourself why he does this. The answer: to show that even the best of them produce weak reasoning.
This makes the refutation more powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لزمهم أن يكون المعنى الذي زعموا أنه يوجب كون الباري عز وجل عالما مثل المعنى الذي يوجب كوننا عالمين، وفي ذلك استحالة قدمه مع كون علومنا محدثة لأن المثلين يستحيل أن يكون أحدهما قديما والآخر محدثا، وذلك أنه إذا كان أحدهما مثلا للآخر وجب لأحدهما من القدم ما وجب للآخر. وهذا كله كلام حكيته بلفظه، وهو ممن تزعم المعتزلة أن علوم سلفهم انتهت إليه، ثم أربى على جميع من تقدم منهم بحدة خاطره وجودة قريحته وقوة فطنته، مع هذا على البحث والتنقير، والتأمل والتفكر، والوقوف على ما عجز عنه من تقدمه منهم، ليعلم من وقف على وهاء كلامه وضعف شبهته وهذه حالته عندهم بطلان مذهبهم وضعف شبههم، وكذبهم في دعاويهم العريضة، وعدولهم عن الصواب فيما يعتقدونه ويذهبون إليه.
It necessarily follows for them that the attribute which they claimed necessitates that the Creator, mighty and majestic, be knowing is similar to the attribute that necessitates that we be knowing. In this is the impossibility of His eternity while our knowledges are created, because it is impossible for two similars to be one eternal and the other created. This is because if one of them is similar to the other, then what is required for one in terms of eternity is required for the other. All of this is speech I have narrated in his own wording. He is one whom the Mu'tazila claim that the knowledges of their predecessors ended with him. Then he surpassed all of those before him in sharpness of mind, excellence of intellect, and strength of cleverness, along with this, persistence in investigation and scrutiny, reflection and contemplation, and grasping what those before him among them were incapable of, so that whoever examines the flimsiness of his speech and the weakness of his argument – and this is his state according to them – realizes the invalidity of their school, the weakness of their doubts, their lies in their extravagant claims, and their deviation from the truth in what they believe and follow.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage introduces the opponent's argument and then dismisses his status. It begins by stating the opponent's claim: the attribute that makes God knowing is similar to the attribute that makes us knowing, which would lead to impossibility because two similar things cannot be one eternal and one created. The author then notes that he has quoted the opponent's own words. He describes the opponent as a leading Mu'tazili figure who surpassed his predecessors in intelligence and scholarship, yet despite this, his argument is flimsy. The purpose is to show that even the best of the Mu'tazila produce weak reasoning, thereby exposing the falsehood of their entire school. The passage sets the stage for the detailed refutation to follow.

Key Terms
المعنى
المثلين
قدم
العلوم
الوهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili argument about God's knowledge being similar to human knowledge is logically sound and must be taken seriously.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the argument leads to a logical impossibility (two similars cannot be one eternal and one created), so it is unsound. Moreover, even their best scholar's version is flimsy, proving the school's error.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim would be impossible if God's attribute of knowledge were similar to human knowledge?
A) God's knowledge would be created like ours.
B) Two similars cannot be one eternal and one created.
C) God would not be knowing.
D) Human knowledge would become eternal.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili analogy of divine and human knowledge by showing it violates the principle of similars, then uses their own champion's weak argument to prove the school's falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge, do you unconsciously measure it by your own understanding? How does Nasafi's argument force you to rethink the relationship between your knowledge and God's, and what practical step can you take today to remind yourself of this difference?
?
The Mu'tazili scholar was considered the best of his time, yet his argument was fundamentally flawed. Are you aware of any authorities you follow whose reasoning might be weak? What specific teaching from this lesson will you use to evaluate their claims?
?
The principle of similars shows that if two things are truly similar, they cannot differ in eternity. Have you ever treated two things as similar when they are actually fundamentally different? How will this principle change the way you approach comparisons in your faith and life?

Mu'tazilite Views on the Qur'an and God's Speech
Nasafi enumerates diverse Mu'tazilite opinions on the createdness and nature of the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diversity of Mu'tazilite opinions on the nature of the Qur'an and the common principle that defines God as 'speaker' (mutakallim) among them.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites disagreed on the Qur'an's essence but agreed that God speaks by creating speech.

The Mu'tazila are often portrayed as a unified school, but their views on the Qur'an reveal significant internal variation. Imam al-Nasafi presents several positions, from the Qur'an as created letters in the Preserved Tablet to a physical body. Despite these differences, they share a fundamental principle about what it means for God to be mutakallim. This passage examines these views and sets the stage for the Sunni critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains multiple opinions attributed to specific Mu'tazilite figures.
2
Identify the different views
List the distinct Mu'tazilite opinions mentioned: Ja'far ibn Harb and Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi, and al-Nazzam. Note what each says about the nature of the Qur'an.
The common principle at the end unites them.
3
Understand the common principle
Focus on the final sentence: according to all these groups, Allah is mutakallim because He creates speech, and the mutakallim is the creator of speech. This is the shared definition they all accept.
This definition is key to understanding the Sunni counter-argument in the following lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في بعض الكتب منسوبا إلى الإسكافي أحد رؤسائهم. فأما المتأخرون من المتكلمين فإنهم ينسبونه إلى الجبائي، ولعل الجبائي اتبع الإسكافي في ذلك. وزعم جعفر بن حرب وجعفر بن مبشر ومن تابعهما من المعتزلة : أن القرآن خلقه الله تعالى في اللوح المحفوظ، ولا يجوز أن ينقل هذا يسمع عنه، وأنه لا يتصور وجوده إلا في مكان واحد عند اتحاد الزمان، وقالوا مع أن القرآن في المصاحف وفي صدور المؤمنين، وإن ما يقرأ أو يسمع من القارئ هو القرآن على ما عليه أكثر الأمة، إلا أنهم ذهبوا في معنى هذا إلى ما يكتب ويحفظ حكاية القرآن وهو فعل الكاتب أو القارئ أو الحافظ، وأن المحكي بحيث خلقه الله تعالى، وإلى هذا القول ذهب أبو القاسم الكعبي ومن تابعه من معتزلة بغداد فكان كلامه تعالى عند هؤلاء في الحقيقة هو الحروف المصورة المكتوبة في اللوح المحفوظ. ومنهم من يقول : إن القرآن جسم وهو النظام، فإن من مذهبه أن الكلام في الشاهد جسم لأن عنده لا عرض إلا الحركة (؟)، ثم عند هؤلاء كلهم الله تعالى متكلم لأنه خلق الكلام، والمتكلم عندهم هو الخالق للكلام، وهو آمر ناه لأنه خلق الأمر والنهي.
The saying concerning some books attributed to al-Iskafi, one of their leaders. As for the later mutakallimūn, they attribute it to al-Jubbā'ī, and perhaps al-Jubbā'ī followed al-Iskāfī in that. Ja‘far ibn Ḥarb, Ja‘far ibn Mubashshir, and those who followed them from the Mu‘tazila claimed that the Qur’ān is created by Allah in the Preserved Tablet, and that it is not permissible for this to be transmitted? that it is heard from Him, and that it cannot be conceived to exist except in one place at a single time. And they said, while the Qur’ān is in the muṣḥafs and in the breasts of the believers, and that what is recited or heard from the reciter is the Qur’ān according to what the majority of the community hold, except that they interpreted this to mean that what is written, memorized, and preserved is a ḥikāya (imitation) of the Qur’ān, which is the act of the writer, reciter, or memorizer, and that the imitated (maḥkī) is as Allah created it. To this opinion went Abū al-Qāsim al-Ka‘bī and those of the Mu‘tazila of Baghdad who followed him, so the speech of Allah according to these people is in reality the letter-shapes written in the Preserved Tablet. And among them is one who says that the Qur’ān is a body, namely al-Naẓẓām, for his doctrine is that speech in the witnessed world is a body, because according to him there is no accident except motion (?). Then according to all of these, Allah is mutakallim because He created speech, and the mutakallim according to them is the creator of speech, and He is a commander and prohibitor because He created command and prohibition.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of various Mu‘tazilite views on the nature of the Qur’ān and how God can be described as ‘speaking’ (mutakallim). Nasafi begins by noting a report that some works attribute a certain opinion to al-Iskāfī, a leading Mu‘tazilite, but later mutakallimūn attribute it to al-Jubbā’ī, suggesting that al-Jubbā’ī may have followed al-Iskāfī. 

He then presents the view of Ja‘far ibn Ḥarb, Ja‘far ibn Mubashshir, and their followers: that Allah created the Qur’ān in the Preserved Tablet, and it cannot be transmitted or heard from Him? and it can only exist in one place at one time. They still affirm that the Qur’ān is in muṣḥafs and in the hearts of believers, but interpret what is recited as a ḥikāya (imitation) of the Qur’ān, not the actual created speech. Abū al-Qāsim al-Ka‘bī and the Baghdad Mu‘tazila held a similar view, identifying God’s speech with the letter-shapes in the Preserved Tablet. 

Next, Nasafi mentions al-Naẓẓām’s view that the Qur’ān is a body (jism), because he held that only motion is an accident, and speech is therefore a body. 

Finally, he summarizes the common principle among all these Mu‘tazilites: they agree that Allah is mutakallim because He creates speech, and the mutakallim is the creator of speech, and He is a commander and prohibitor because He creates command and prohibition. This sets the stage for the next passage where some Mu‘tazilites cannot even affirm that.

Key Terms
المتكلم
القرآن
الحكاية
اللوح المحفوظ
الجسم
النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume the Mu'tazila were unanimous on the nature of the Qur'an.
Correction: This passage shows significant internal diversity: some say it is created letters, others a body, and they differ on whether the recited text is the Qur'an itself or an imitation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the common principle among the Mu'tazilite groups mentioned regarding the meaning of 'mutakallim'?
A) He is called mutakallim because speech is an eternal attribute of His essence.
B) He is called mutakallim because He creates speech, and the mutakallim is the creator of speech.
C) He is called mutakallim because He has a body that speaks.
D) He is called mutakallim because He recites the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites held different views on the Qur'an's nature but agreed that God is mutakallim because He creates speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu‘tazila debated the nature of the Qur’ān, but you hold it in your hands daily. Do you treat it as the very speech of Allah, or just a book of guidance? What would change if you truly believed it is His uncreated word?
?
They reduced God’s speech to a creation – a thing He made. When you read the Qur’ān, do you sense the presence of the Speaker, or do you merely process words? How can you train your heart to hear the Creator behind the verses?
?
Al-Naẓẓām said the Qur’ān is a body – a physical thing. You may never say that, but do you treat the Qur’ān as a mere object on a shelf, or as a living message that demands your immediate obedience? What is one verse you have ignored that you will obey starting today?

Why Three Mu'tazilites Cannot Affirm God as Mutakallim
Nasafi shows why three Mu'tazilites cannot affirm God as mutakallim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the internal principles of three Mu'tazilite figures (al-Asamm, Mu'ammar, and Thumama) logically prevented them from affirming that God is mutakallim, and why this has devastating theological consequences.

Core Idea
If you deny God's speech, you deny the Qur'an and all divine commands.

Nasafi continues his critique of Mu'tazilite positions on the divine attribute of speech. He now targets three specific figures whose own doctrines trap them in a logical dead end: they cannot affirm that God speaks at all. As you read, observe how each figure's unique principles lead to the same result—a denial of God as mutakallim—and how Nasafi draws out the catastrophic consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three figures
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the pattern: each figure is introduced, then shown unable to accept either the Ash'ari-Maturidi or the mainstream Mu'tazilite position.
2
Understand al-Asamm's dilemma
Why does al-Asamm reject both eternal speech and created speech? Hint: he denies both attributes and accidents.
3
Understand Mu'ammar's dilemma
What is unique about Mu'ammar's position on accidents? How does it prevent him from accepting God creating speech?
4
Understand Thumama's dilemma
Thumama holds that speech is generated (tawallud) from movement of organs. Why does that make it impossible for God to speak?
5
Reflect on the consequences
Nasafi draws severe conclusions: denial of God as speaker leads to denial of Qur'an and all divine law. Think about why that is such a serious charge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبو بكر الأصم من جملتهم لا يمكنه أن يقول إن الله متكلم، لأن عنده يستحيل كونه متكلما بكلام أزلي قائم بذاته لإنكاره الصفات، ولا يمكنه أن يقول إنه متكلم بخلقه الكلام في محل كما هو مذهب إخوانه من المعتزلة، لأن الكلام الحادث عرض وهو ينفي الأعراض. ومعمر بن عباد السلمي شيخهم المقدم لا يمكنه أن يقول : إنه تعالى متكلم بكلام أزلي قائم به كما يقوله أهل الحق، لإنكاره الصفات، ولا يمكنه أن يقول إنه متكلم بخلقه الكلام كما يقول إخوانه من المعتزلة، لأن الكلام الحادث عرض وهو يقول: لا قدرة الله تعالى على تخليق شيء من الأعراض بل خالقها محالها إما باختيارها وإما طباعها، وكذا ثمامة بن الأشرس النميري تلميذ النظام لا يمكنه أن يصف الله تعالى بأنه متكلم لا كما يقول أهل الحق، لإنكاره الصفات ولا كما يقوله المعتزلة لأن الكلام الحادث عنده يوجب بطريق التولد من تحريك المتكلم الآلات التي بها يتكلم، ومن مذهبه أن المتولدات أفعال لا فاعل لها. فعلى قول هؤلاء القلة الثلاثة ليس الله تعالى بمتكلم ولا آمر ولا ناه، وأن القرآن ليس بكلامه، وفي هذا تكذيب محمد عليه السلام بقوله إن هذا كلام الله تعالى، ورفع الشرائع، وإبطال الفرض والوجوب والحظر لثبوت ذلك كله بأمره ونهيه ولا أمر ولا نهي عندهم، ولشياطينهم أقاويل تستنكف البهائم أن تنسب إليها، ولولا مخافة التطويل لحكيتها
And Abū Bakr al-Aṣamm is among them; he cannot say that Allah is mutakallim, because according to him it is impossible for Him to be mutakallim with eternal speech subsisting in His essence due to his denial of attributes, and he cannot say that He is mutakallim by creating speech in a locus as is the doctrine of his brethren from the Mu‘tazila, because originated speech is an accident and he denies accidents. And Mu‘ammar ibn ‘Abbād al-Sulamī, their leading elder, cannot say that He, exalted, is mutakallim with eternal speech subsisting in Him as the people of truth say, due to his denial of attributes, and he cannot say that He is mutakallim by creating speech as his brethren from the Mu‘tazila say, because originated speech is an accident, and he says: Allah, exalted, has no power to create any accident; rather their creator is their locus, either by its choice or by its nature. Likewise Thumāma ibn Ashras al-Numayrī, the student of al-Naẓẓām, cannot describe Allah as mutakallim, neither as the people of truth say, due to his denial of attributes, nor as the Mu‘tazila say, because originated speech according to him necessitates by way of generation (tawallud) from the speaker's movement of the instruments by which he speaks, and his doctrine is that generated things are actions without an agent. So according to these three individuals, Allah is not mutakallim, nor a commander, nor a prohibitor, and the Qur’ān is not His speech. And in this is the falsification of Muḥammad (peace be upon him) in his saying 'This is the speech of Allah,' and the abrogation of the divine laws, and the nullification of obligation, necessity, and prohibition, since all that is established only by His command and prohibition, and they have no command or prohibition. And their devils have sayings that animals would disdain to have attributed to them, and were it not for fear of prolixity I would have related them.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument showing that three specific Mu‘tazilites—Abū Bakr al-Aṣamm, Mu‘ammar ibn ‘Abbād al-Sulamī, and Thumāma ibn Ashras—are logically compelled by their own principles to deny that God is mutakallim, which has devastating theological consequences. Nasafi proceeds figure by figure.

First, Abū Bakr al-Aṣamm: He cannot affirm God as mutakallim with eternal speech because he denies eternal attributes. Nor can he affirm God as mutakallim by creating speech in a locus (as other Mu‘tazila do), because he denies accidents altogether, and originated speech is an accident. Thus, he has no way to say God speaks.

Second, Mu‘ammar ibn ‘Abbād: He too denies attributes, so he cannot accept eternal speech. He also cannot accept God's creating speech as an accident, because he holds that God cannot create accidents; rather, accidents are created by their own locus, either by choice or by nature. So he cannot say God is the creator of speech.

Third, Thumāma ibn Ashras: He also denies attributes. He cannot accept the Mu‘tazilite position because he holds that speech is generated (tawallud) from the movement of the speaker's organs, and generated acts have no agent. Thus, if God were to speak, it would require a body, which is impossible, and the generated speech would be agentless.

Nasafi then draws the conclusion: according to these three, God is not mutakallim, not a commander, not a prohibitor, and the Qur’ān is not His speech. This entails falsifying the Prophet Muhammad's statement that the Qur’ān is God's speech, abolishing all divine laws, obligations, and prohibitions, since these depend on God's command and prohibition, which they deny. The passage ends with a sharp condemnation of their further heretical sayings, which he refrains from detailing only to avoid excessive length.

معمر بن عباد

Correction: This passage shows that three prominent Mu'tazilites—al-Asamm, Mu'ammar, and Thumama—were logically unable to affirm that God is mutakallim due to their own principles.

Checkpoint

C) He believes speech is generated by the movement of organs.
D) He believes the Qur'an is uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The internal logic of al-Asamm, Mu'ammar, and Thumama compels them to deny that God is mutakallim, which undermines the Qur'an and all divine commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe the Qur'an is God's speech, how does that demand a change in the way you approach it daily—not just in recitation, but in obedience to every command and prohibition? What specific command or prohibition have you been neglecting that you need to act on today?
?
The three figures ended up denying God's command and prohibition because of their theological commitments. Have you ever allowed a desire or opinion to subtly override a clear command from the Qur'an, effectively acting as if God's command does not apply? Confess that to yourself and resolve to submit.

Mu'tazila's Self-Contradiction on Tawhid and Justice
The author shows how Mu'tazila's denial of attributes undermines their own claims to tawhid and justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila's denial of divine attributes and their affirmation of human power to create actions lead to contradictions in their own stated principles of tawhid and justice.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's doctrines undermine their own claims to tawhid and justice.

The Mu'tazila prided themselves on being the people of tawhid and justice. Yet, as the author demonstrates, their core theological positions—negating God's attributes and affirming that humans create their own actions—actually contradict these very claims. This passage exposes the internal inconsistency of their system.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila's two claims
The Mu'tazila call themselves 'People of Tawhid' for negating attributes, and 'People of Justice' for affirming that humans create their own actions. Recognize what each claim means.
They define tawhid as affirming a single pre-eternal existent without attributes. They define justice as God not creating evil acts.
2
See how negating attributes undermines tawhid
If God does not create actions, then actions must be created by others. Since the world includes voluntary actions, multiple creators would exist, destroying tawhid.
The author argues that if actions are not created by God, then there are countless creators.
3
See how affirming human power undermines justice
If humans create their own actions, then God would be punishing them for acts He did not create. This would make God a 'torturer without crime', which is unjust.
Justice requires that punishment be for one's own deed; if God does not create the deed, He cannot justly punish.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the Mu'tazila have nullified their justice by their tawhid, and their tawhid by their justice. This shows the internal contradiction.
The two doctrines are mutually destructive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتزلة وقولهم بالعدل والتوحيد] ثم هم بنفي الصفات يسمون أنفسهم أهل التوحيد يعنون أنهم يثبتون في الأزل موجودا واحدا، وقد أبطلوا عدل الله تعالى حيث صار معذبا من غير جريمة على ما قررت وبإثبات قدرة تخليق الأفعال للعباد يسمون أنفسهم أهل عدل، لأن تخليق الأفعال لو كان من الله تعالى لكان معذبا لهم على فعله لا على فعلهم، وقد أبطلوا بهذا توحيد الصانع العالم. إذ العالم أعيان وأعراض، وأكثر الأعراض التي الأفعال الاختيارية لما دب ودرج وجد لا بتخليق الله تعالى، فكان العالم مخلوقا الله تعالى ولمن لا يحصون من الخالقين وهذا إبطال التوحيد ولهذا قال أصحابنا : إن المعتزلة يسمون أنفسهم أهل عدل وتوحيد، وقد أبطلوا عدلهم بتوحيدهم وتوحيدهم بعد لهم، وهذا ظاهر بحمد الله.
The Mu'tazila and their claim to justice and tawhid. Then they, by negating attributes, call themselves people of tawhid, meaning that they affirm in pre-eternity a single existent. And they have nullified the justice of God Most High where He becomes a torturer without crime, as I have settled. And by affirming a power to create actions for servants, they call themselves people of justice, because if the creation of actions were from God Most High, He would be torturing them for His action, not for their action. And by this they have nullified the tawhid of the Maker, the Knower. For the world consists of substances and accidents, and most of the accidents that are voluntary actions, since they came about, existed not by the creation of God Most High. So the world becomes created by God Most High and by countless creators. And this is nullification of tawhid. And because of this our companions said: The Mu'tazila call themselves people of justice and tawhid, but they have nullified their justice by their tawhid, and their tawhid by their justice. And this is clear, praise be to God.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazila for using the terms 'tawhid' and 'justice' while their doctrines contradict them. They claim tawhid by negating attributes, but this leads to multiple creators if actions are not created by God. They claim justice by attributing power to servants, but this implies God punishes for actions He didn't create, which is unjust. Thus they undermine both concepts.

Correction: The passage shows the opposite: negating attributes leads to multiple creators (shirk), thus negating tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why do Mu'tazila nullify their own claim to tawhid?
A) Because they affirm attributes
B) Because they deny that God creates actions, leading to multiple creators
C) Because they deny free will

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's doctrines of tawhid (by negating attributes) and justice (by affirming human power) are internally inconsistent and undermine each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God's justice but ended up undermining His oneness. Is there a belief you hold about God that you think protects His justice but might actually compromise His sovereignty? Are you willing to re-examine it in light of this lesson?
?
If you believe that God is just, how do you reconcile that with the evil in the world? The Mu'tazila's solution was to deny God's creation of actions. After seeing their self-contradiction, can you instead trust that God's wisdom decrees evil for a greater good, even if you don't understand it?
?
The author says the contradiction is 'clear, praise be to God'. Could it be that you have been unknowingly holding contradictory beliefs about Allah? Will you humbly return to the revealed texts and the understanding of the righteous predecessors, even if it challenges your intellect?

Focusing on Relevant Objections
Nasafi explains why he omits most Mu'tazili objections and limits discussion to relevant ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi limits his discussion of Mu'tazili objections to only those that directly apply to his Maturidi position, and how this reflects a broader methodological principle.

Core Idea
Nasafi addresses only objections that target his own position, not those aimed at others.

Nasafi has just presented a three-part division of views on divine speech. Now he explains a crucial methodological choice: although the Mu'tazila raise many objections, most of them are aimed at the Ash'ariyya, not at his own Maturidi school. Therefore, he will only mention those objections that actually apply to his position. This tells us something important about how classical theologians engaged with their opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the three divisions mentioned: the last division (Mu'tazila), the first division (Karramiyya), and the second division (Abu al-Hudhayl).
The passage is brief but packs a lot of information.
2
Identify why most objections are ignored
The author states that most of the questions (objections) are directed at the Ash'ariyya because they agree with the Mu'tazila on the temporal origination of attributes of action. Since these objections do not apply to his own position, he refrains from mentioning them.
This shows the author's focus on relevant debate.
3
Reflect on the methodology
Ask yourself: what does this choice reveal about the author's approach? He is not avoiding difficult questions; he is being efficient by addressing only objections that directly challenge his own views.
This is a common practice in theological works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام آخر للمعتزلة] ولهم على هذا القسم الأخير أسئلة كثيرة، إذ هو مذهبهم كلهم، فأما القسم الأول: فهو قول الكرامية، والقسم الثاني: مذهبا أبي الهذيل وحده في خطاب "كن" خاصة لا فيما وراءه من الكلام إلا أن أكثر تلك الأسئلة يتوجه على الأشعرية لمساعدتهم المعتزلة في حدوث صفات الفعل، ولا يتوجه علينا فأعرضنا عن ذكرها، وذكرنا ما لهم علينا من الأسئلة.
Another statement regarding the Mu'tazila. They have many questions regarding this last division, for it is their school entirely. As for the first division, it is the statement of the Karramiyya. And the second division is the school of Abu al-Hudhayl alone regarding the address 'Be' specifically, not beyond that of speech, except that most of those questions are directed at the Ash'ariyya due to their agreement with the Mu'tazila on the temporal origination of attributes of action, and they are not directed at us, so we refrain from mentioning them; we mention only the questions they have against us.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage provides a taxonomy of positions on divine speech. The 'last division' likely refers to the Mu'tazili position that speech is created. The Karramiyya hold speech can be originated in God's essence (first division). Abu al-Hudhayl's unique view on the word 'Be' (second division). Most objections from Mu'tazila target Ash'ariyya because Ash'ariyya agree with Mu'tazila on the createdness of attributes of action, so the author specifically addresses only those objections that apply to his own Maturidi position.

?
The passage shows that even within Sunni Islam there is a significant difference on divine attributes. Have you ever assumed that everyone in your own tradition agrees on everything? What might this cost you in terms of learning from diversity?
