Al-Razi's Objection and Nasafi's Dialectical Refutation on Speech
Al-Razi's objection to speech as attribute and Nasafi's dialectical refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection to speech as an inherent attribute and Nasafi's dialectical response that turns the objection back on itself.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's own logic, if consistent, would invalidate his own position.

This passage presents a heated exchange between the Mu'tazili scholar 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi and the Maturidi Imam al-Nasafi over the nature of divine speech. Al-Razi argues that speech cannot be an attribute inhering in the speaker, offering several reductio arguments. Nasafi first replies with a dialectical (jadali) refutation, showing that al-Razi's principles lead to absurdities in his own system. Then he hints at the real answer, which ties back to earlier discussions on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's objection carefully
Identify each of al-Razi's arguments: (1) speech does not pervade the whole living being, so the whole cannot be a speaker; (2) if speech inheres in a part, the tongue would be the speaker; (3) truthfulness and blame would attach to the tongue; (4) each letter is specific to a location, so no single part can be a speaker; (5) the epileptic example shows speech can come from a jinn, not the person.
Notice that al-Razi uses multiple reductio ad absurdum arguments to support his view that speech is an action, not an attribute.
2
Follow Nasafi's dialectical response
Nasafi first points out that just as speech does not pervade the whole body, neither does the whole body perform speech (e.g., skin, bones). Yet al-Razi still calls the whole person a speaker. This shows inconsistency, because according to al-Razi's own criterion (speaker = doer of speech), most body parts do no speech, so the whole cannot be a speaker.
This is a classic tu quoque (you too) argument: al-Razi's objection applies equally to his own position.
3
Understand the 'real answer' reference
Nasafi indicates that the real answer involves the early scholars' view that the entire essence is described by an attribute even if it exists only in a part, because God makes the entirety one thing. The text cuts off here, so this will be completed later.
The dialectical answer suffices to show the flaw in al-Razi's reasoning, but the deeper answer is based on the unity of the essence.
4
Reflect on the dialectical method
Consider how Nasafi uses al-Razi's own premises against him. This is a characteristic method in kalam debates: showing that an opponent's argument, if applied consistently, destroys their own position.
This does not prove the Maturidi view directly, but it removes the objection and creates space for the positive argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جملة هذه الأسئلة ما ذكره عبد الجبار الرازي وهو عندهم أعلم أهل نحلته فزعم أن الحي منا إنما كان متكلما لأنه فعل الكلام لا لأن الكلام قام به بدليل أن الكلام لا يحل جملة الحي فكان يجب أن يستحيل أن يكون متكلما قال فإن قال هلا كان كذلك بأن حل في بعضه قال فجوابنا أنه كان يجب أن يكون اللسان متكلما دون الإنسان لأن الكلام ببعضه أخص قال وكان يجب أن لا يكون الصادق بالصدق الإنسان ولا يتعلق الذم والمدح به قال وكان يجب أن يكون كل محل من اللسان متكلما إن كان المتكلم ما حل به الكلام وكان يجب إذا كان كل حرف يختص بمحل منه أن لا يستقر كون شيء من ذلك متكلما لأن الحرف الواحد لا يكون كلاما قال ويبين ذلك أنهم يقولون في المصروع إذا تكلم بحكمة إن الجني يتكلم على لسانه لما اعتقدوا في ذلك القول أنه ليس من قبله قال وذلك يبطل قول من قال إنما صار متكلما لظهور الكلام منه ووجوده في لسانه هذا هو سؤاله في المسألة فنقول في جوابه والله الموفق إنه جمع في كلامه أنواعا من الهذيان والمغالطة يظهر ذلك عند الكشف من ذلك أنه قال إن الكلام لا يحل جملة الحي فكان يجب أن يستحيل أن يكون كله متكلما قلنا له والكلام لا يفعله جملة الحي فإن جلده وعظمه وأحشاءه وأمعاءه وأصابعه وأظفاره لا توصف بتحصيلها الكلام فكان يستحيل أن يكون كله متكلما ولما جاز له أن يجعل كل الذات متكلما وإن لم يفعل الكلام لما بينا من هذه الأجزاء مع أن المتكلم عنده فاعل الكلام لماذا لا يجوز لخصمه أن يجعل كل الذات متكلما وإن لم يقم الكلام بجميع أجزائه فيلزمه عين ما ألزم خصمه والتعلق بما يفضي ببطلان مذهبه حسب قضائه ببطلان مذهب خصمه درب من السفه وعدول عن سنن المجادلة هذا هو الجواب الجدلي فأما حقيقة الجواب فهو ما مر في مسألة الصفات من بيان الاختلاف بين قدماء أصحاب الصفات وبين متأخريهم فإن عند القدماء كل الذات يكون موصوفا وإن وجدت الصفة ببعضه لما أن كل الذات جعل شيئا واحدا بجعل جاعل وعند
And among these questions is what 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi mentioned—and he is according to them the most learned of his sect—and he claimed that the living being among us is only a speaker because he performed the speech, not because speech inhered in him, as evidenced by the fact that speech does not pervade the whole living being, so it would be impossible for the whole to be a speaker. He said: if one says: why is it not so, because (speech) inheres in some part of him? He said: our answer is that then the tongue should be the speaker, not the human, because speech is specific to some part. He said: and it would be necessary that the truthful person not be the human through truthfulness, and praise and blame would not pertain to him. He said: and it would be necessary that every location in the tongue be a speaker if the speaker is that in which speech inheres, and it would be necessary that if every letter is specific to a location in it, then nothing of that would be a speaker because a single letter is not speech. He said: and that is clarified by the fact that they say about an epileptic, if he speaks wisely, that the jinn speaks upon his tongue, because they believe that speech is not from him. He said: and that invalidates the statement of one who says that one becomes a speaker only because of the appearance of speech from him and its existence in his tongue. This is his question in this matter. Then we say in answer, and God is the grantor of success: he has collected in his speech types of raving and sophistry; that becomes clear upon investigation. Among that is his statement: speech does not pervade the whole living being, so it would be impossible for the whole to be a speaker. We say to him: speech is not performed by the whole living being, for his skin, bone, intestines, bowels, fingers, and nails are not described as producing speech, so it would be impossible for the whole to be a speaker. Since he allowed himself to make the whole essence a speaker even though it does not perform speech, as we have shown regarding these parts—while in his view the speaker is the doer of speech—why is it not permissible for his opponent to make the whole essence a speaker even though speech does not inhere in all its parts? Then the very same thing that he forced upon his opponent would be required of him. And clinging to what leads to the invalidity of his own doctrine, according to his judgment of the invalidity of his opponent's doctrine, is a path of foolishness and a deviation from the way of debate. This is the dialectical answer. As for the real answer, it is what has passed in the question of attributes concerning the clarification of the difference between the early scholars of attributes and their later ones. For according to the early ones, the whole essence is described even if the attribute exists in part of it, because the whole essence is made one thing by the making of a maker. And
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage contains first the full objection of 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi, a leading Mu'tazili scholar, followed by Nasafi's two-step refutation. Al-Razi argues that a living being is a speaker because he performs speech, not because speech inheres in him. He offers several reductio points: if speech inhered only in a part (the tongue), then the tongue alone would be the speaker, not the human; truthfulness and blame would attach to the tongue; and since each letter is distinct, no single part could be a speaker. He also cites the example of an epileptic speaking wisely, attributing the speech to a jinn, to show that the speaker is not necessarily the one from whom speech appears. Nasafi first gives a dialectical (jadali) response: if al-Razi claims speech does not pervade the whole body, then by the same logic the whole body cannot be said to perform speech (since organs like skin and bones do not produce speech). Yet al-Razi still calls the whole person a speaker despite speech not being performed by all parts; thus his own principle is violated. Nasafi then outlines the real (haqiqa) answer: the early scholars (qudama') of attributes held that the entire essence is described with an attribute even if the attribute exists only in part of it, because the entirety is made one by God's making (ja'l). The text is cut off at 'and' (wa 'inda), but the argument is that this unified entity is truly the subject of the attribute.

فعل الكلام
قام به
محل
لسان

جني
أصحاب الصفات
جعل جاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Razi's objection decisively disproves the Maturidi view that speech is an attribute inhering in God.
Correction: Nasafi demonstrates that al-Razi's reasoning is self-defeating: it would also invalidate al-Razi's own theory that speech is an act. The dialectical answer shows the objection is not conclusive.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's objection, what follows if speech inheres only in a part of the body (e.g., the tongue)?
A) The whole person would still be considered the speaker.
B) The tongue would be the speaker, not the human.
C) Speech would be eternal.
D) The jinn would be responsible for all speech.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's objection fails on its own terms; Nasafi's dialectical refutation exposes a self-contradiction, and the real answer rests on the unity of the essence through God's making.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi's objection was self-defeating because he used a standard that applied equally to his own view. When you oppose a belief, do you hold yourself to the same logical standards? Think of a time when you criticized others but overlooked the same flaw in yourself. Will you commit to self-examination before arguing against others?
?
Nasafi calls al-Razi's clinging to a position that invalidates his own doctrine 'a path of foolishness.' Are there areas in your own faith where you stubbornly hold onto a position despite evidence that it contradicts other things you believe? What would it take for you to let go and seek the truth?
?
The 'real answer' rests on the unity of the essence through God's making. Do you truly live as if your entire being—every part—is unified under God's sovereignty, or do you compartmentalize parts of your life where you act independently? How can you submit the whole of your existence to the One who made you one?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
Al-Basri's Objection on Speech Opposites
Al-Basri's objection that muteness and silence are not opposites of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's objection that muteness and silence are not proper opposites of speech, and the reasoning behind his claim.

Core Idea
Muteness is a corruption of the speech organ, not an opposite of speech.

Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, a prominent Mu'tazilite theologian, raises a nuanced objection against the standard Maturidi position that muteness and silence are opposites of speech. He argues that muteness is a physical defect, silence is the abandonment of using an organ, and neither can apply to God. This lesson presents his objection in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage of the objection once, then the translation. Notice how al-Basri structures his argument: he first denies that muteness and silence are opposites, then defines them in terms of an organ.
The objection cites a hadith about the Quran's silence, which al-Basri uses to support describing God as silent.
2
Identify the three claims
Al-Basri makes three distinct claims: (1) Muteness is a corruption of the speech organ, not an opposite; (2) Silence is abandonment of using the organ; (3) Since God cannot have an organ, He cannot be described as silent or speaking.
These claims challenge the very notion of speech as an attribute of God.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why al-Basri's objection would be significant for the Maturidi position. What does it mean if muteness and silence are not opposites? How does the organ-based argument threaten divine speech?
The response in the next lesson will address these points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض آخر لأبي الحسين البصري:
واعترض أبو الحسين البصري على هذه الطريقة فزعم أن الخرس والسكوت ليسا بضدين للكلام إنما الخرس هو فساد آلة الكلام، فلا يكون ضد الكلام، كالزمانة ليست بضد للفعل، وبخروجه من أن يكون فاعلا لم يلزم أن يكون زمنا، والسكوت ترك استعمال آلة الكلام، والله تعالى يستحيل عليه الآلة فيستحيل وصفه بأنه ساكت قال على أن عند بعض أصحابنا يجوز وصف الله تعالى بأنه ساكت قبل أن يتكلم ، وقد روي عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال: "ما سكت عنه القرآن فهو عفو."
الرد على اعتراضه
فيجاب عن هذا الاعتراض فيقال: هل تقر أن الخرس ينافي الكلام؟ فإن قال: لا بَانَ بهته، وكلف أن يجوز اجتماع الكلام والخرس وإن قال : هو ينافي الكلام. فهذا هو الإقرار بكونهما متضادين وكذا الزمانة عندنا ضد الفعل لما قررنا، والاستشهاد بالزمانة إنما يستقيم له على الأشعرية لا علينا على أنه إن كان لا يسمى ضدا فلا شك أنه مانع، فيمتنع وجود الكلام عند وجوده عن من لا يستحيل اتصافه بالكلام، وعند ارتفاعه وارتفاع السكوت يجب وجود الكلام على ما قررنا. وكذا ما ذكر أن السكوت ليس بضد للكلام ، (ويجاب عنه) بما ذكر في فصل الخرس، ثم يقال له تنفصل ممن يقول لك : إن الكلام هو استعمال آلة الكلام، كما أن السكوت ترك استعمال آلة الكلام، والخرس آفة في آلة الكلام، والله سبحانه وتعالى يستحيل عليه الخرس والسكوت لاستحالة ثبوت الآلة له، فكذا يستحيل عليه الاتصاف بالكلام لاستحالة كونه ذا آلة وحيث جاز الاتصاف بالكلام مع
A new objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri:
Abu al-Husayn al-Basri objected to this method and claimed that muteness (al-khars) and silence (al-sukut) are not opposites of speech. Rather, muteness is a corruption of the organ of speech, so it is not an opposite of speech, just as lameness (al-zamanah) is not an opposite of action, and by his being unable to act, it does not entail that he is lame. And silence is the abandonment of using the organ of speech. And Allah, exalted, it is impossible for Him to have an organ (aala). Therefore it is impossible to describe Him as silent. He said: However, according to some of our companions, it is permissible to describe Allah, exalted, as silent before He speaks, and it has been narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'What the Quran is silent about, it is pardon.'
The response to his objection:
It is responded to this objection by saying: Do you affirm that muteness contradicts speech? If he says: No, then his absurdity becomes clear, and he would be required to allow the coexistence of speech and muteness. And if he says: It contradicts speech, then this is an admission that they are opposites. And likewise lameness, according to us, is an opposite of action, as we have established. And citing lameness is only valid for him against the Ash'ari, not against us. Even if it is not called an opposite, there is no doubt it is a preventative (mani'). So the existence of speech is impossible when it (muteness) is present from one for whom it is not impossible to be described with speech. And when it (muteness) is removed and silence is removed, speech must exist, as we have established. And likewise what he mentioned that silence is not an opposite of speech is responded to by what was mentioned in the section on muteness. Then it is said to him: You become separated from the one who says to you: Speech is the use of the organ of speech, just as silence is the abandonment of using the organ of speech, and muteness is an impairment of the organ of speech. And Allah, glorified and exalted, it is impossible for Him to have muteness and silence because it is impossible for Him to have an organ. So likewise, it is impossible for Him to be described with speech because it is impossible for Him to be one having an organ. And since it is permissible to be described with speech with
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage records a specific objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, a Mu'tazilite scholar, and the author's response. The objection challenges the claim that muteness (al-khars) and silence (al-sukut) are opposites of speech. Al-Basri argues that muteness is a corruption of the speech organ, not an opposite, just as lameness is not an opposite of action. Silence is the abandonment of using the organ. Since God cannot have an organ, He cannot be described as silent or speaking. He also notes that some Maturidi companions allow describing God as silent before speech, citing a hadith. The response begins by asking whether muteness contradicts speech. If the opponent denies it, then absurdity follows; if he admits it, then they are opposites. The response also addresses lameness, stating that according to Maturidis it is an opposite of action, and the citation only works against Ash'aris. Even if not technically a 'opposite', muteness is a preventative (mani'). The existence of speech is impossible when muteness is present, and when both muteness and silence are removed, speech must exist. The response then addresses silence, referring back to the section on muteness. Finally, it exposes a flaw in the opponent's reasoning: if speech requires an organ, and God cannot have an organ, then by their logic, God cannot be described with speech either. Yet they affirm speech for God, so their argument is self-refuting. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing on the next page.

استعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muteness and silence are straightforward opposites of speech.
Correction: Al-Basri argues they are not opposites; muteness is a physical defect and silence is an action (abandonment) relative to an organ, not mere absence.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, what is muteness (al-khars)?
A) An opposite of speech.
B) A corruption of the organ of speech.
C) The same as silence.
D) An attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
Al-Basri challenges the Maturidi view by defining muteness and silence in terms of a physical organ, which cannot apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is silent', do you catch yourself imagining Him with a tongue that chooses not to speak? How can you actively purify your mental image of God to match the transcendence taught here?
?
Al-Basri argues that describing God as 'mute' or 'silent' implies a physical organ. Have you ever casually attributed human limitations to God in your prayers or thoughts? What specific change in your vocabulary or reflection can you make today to avoid this?
?
The hadith says 'What the Quran is silent about is pardon.' Are you comfortable with divine silence in matters not explicitly addressed, or do you find yourself seeking absolute answers where the Quran is silent? How does this lesson challenge your approach to uncertainty in faith?

Refuting the Objection: Logic and Premises
Nasafi's response to al-Basri's objection, demonstrating that muteness and silence oppose speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi response to al-Basri's objection, including the use of logical contradiction, the concept of 'preventative' (mani'), and the self-refuting nature of the opponent's argument.

Core Idea
If muteness contradicts speech, it is an opposite; if not, absurdity follows.

After presenting the objection, Nasafi responds with a careful analysis. He forces the opponent to admit that muteness contradicts speech, or else allow absurdities. He also turns the organ-based argument against the opponent, showing that if speech requires an organ, then God cannot be described with speech—which the opponent accepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic response and its translation. Note how Nasafi begins with a direct question to the opponent, forcing him into a dilemma.
The response uses the opponent's own analogy (lameness) and inverts it.
2
Follow the logical structure
The response has several moves: (1) The initial question about contradiction; (2) The lameness analogy revisited; (3) The preventative concept; (4) The organ argument turned against the opponent. Trace each move.
Each move builds on the previous one to refute the objection.
3
Analyze the self-refutation
Pay special attention to the final argument about the organ. Nasafi shows that if speech requires an organ, then by al-Basri's own logic, God cannot speak. Since al-Basri affirms God's speech, his objection collapses.
This is a powerful refutation that exposes an internal contradiction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A new objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri:
Abu al-Husayn al-Basri objected to this method and claimed that muteness (al-khars) and silence (al-sukut) are not opposites of speech. Rather, muteness is a corruption of the organ of speech, so it is not an opposite of speech, just as lameness (al-zamanah) is not an opposite of action, and by his being unable to act, it does not entail that he is lame. He said: However, according to some of our companions, it is permissible to describe Allah, exalted, as silent before He speaks, and it has been narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'What the Quran is silent about, it is pardon.'
The response to his objection:

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue is the consequence if the opponent denies that muteness contradicts speech?
A) The opponent must allow muteness and speech to coexist.

Key Takeaway

When you face a theological objection, do you dismiss it emotionally or do you dissect its logical premises as Nasafi does? What would it cost you to adopt this rigorous method in your own doubts?
?
If God's speech requires no organ, how does that reshape your understanding of how He speaks to you—through the Qur'an, your conscience, or life events? Are you truly listening to a God who speaks without limitation?
?
Nasafi's argument exposes absurdity in the opponent's position. Is there a belief you hold that, under logical scrutiny, would collapse into contradiction? Are you courageous enough to test it?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Accusation on Quranic Revelation
Al-Nasafi defends Maturidism against a Mu'tazilite accusation by exposing a parallel view among Mu'tazilite scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi defends the Maturidi position on the Quran's revelation by highlighting common ground with the Mu'tazila and clarifying the subtle difference between the two schools.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila themselves affirm divine speech in the Preserved Tablet—just like the Maturidis.

This passage addresses a common Mu'tazilite reproach that Maturidis allegedly deny the Quran was revealed to the Prophet Muhammad. Al-Nasafi refutes this by pointing out that leading Mu'tazilite scholars hold a remarkably similar view: they too believe divine speech is in the Preserved Tablet and that the revealed Quran consists of expressions pointing to it. The only disagreement is on what those expressions convey.

Guided Path
1
Understand the accusation
Read the passage to grasp the Mu'tazilite claim that Maturidis say the Quran was not revealed because an attribute cannot separate from its subject.
2
Identify Al-Nasafi's counter-argument
Notice how Al-Nasafi cites specific Mu'tazilite scholars (Ja'far ibn Harb, Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, al-Ka'bi) who also hold that divine speech is in the Preserved Tablet and the revealed Quran are expressions indicating it.
This shows the accusation is unfounded because the Mu'tazila hold a parallel position.
3
Pinpoint the key difference
Examine the final sentence where Al-Nasafi states the distinction: the Mu'tazila say the expressions convey what is in the Tablet, while Maturidis say they convey the attribute subsisting in God's essence.
Both sides agree on the revealed Quran being expressions, but differ on the source of those expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرق بين الماتريدية والمعتزلة] وما شنع علينا بعض أغبيائهم، أنكم تقولون: لم ينزل القرآن على محمد عليه السلام لأن الصفة لا تزايل الموصوف مردود عليهم، لأن جعفر بن حرب وجعفر بن مبشر والكعبي يزعمون أن كلام الله في اللوح المحفوظ والمنزل عبارات دالة عليه على حسب ما نقوله نحن، غير أنهم يقولون: إن هذه العبارات مؤدية ما في اللوح المحفوظ، ونحن نقول: هي مؤدية ما قام بالذات من الصفة.
Difference between the Maturidis and the Mu'tazila. And what some of their ignorant ones have reproached us with, that you say: the Quran was not revealed to Muhammad, peace be upon him, because the attribute does not separate from the described, is refuted against them. For Ja'far ibn Harb, Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, and al-Ka'bi claim that the speech of Allah is in the Preserved Tablet and the revealed are expressions indicating it, according to what we say, except that they say: these expressions convey what is in the Preserved Tablet, while we say: they convey what subsists in the essence of the attribute.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage begins with a section heading indicating a distinction between the Maturidis and the Mu'tazila. Al-Nasafi then addresses a reproach from some ignorant Mu'tazila who accuse the Maturidis of saying that the Quran was not revealed to Prophet Muhammad because an attribute cannot separate from its subject. He refutes this by noting that certain Mu'tazilite scholars (Ja'far ibn Harb, Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, and al-Ka'bi) themselves hold that divine speech is in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz) and that the revealed Quran consists of expressions (ibarat) indicating it. This is similar to the Maturidi view. The only difference is that the Mu'tazila say these expressions convey what is in the Tablet, while the Maturidis say they convey the attribute (al-sifa) that subsists in God's essence. Thus, the accusation is unfounded.

Key Terms

المنزل
عبارات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidis deny that the Quran was revealed to Prophet Muhammad.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the Maturidi position is similar to that of leading Mu'tazilites who affirm the Quran's revelation as expressions indicating divine speech in the Preserved Tablet.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi respond to the Mu'tazilite reproach that the Maturidis say the Quran was not revealed?
A) He denies that any Mu'tazilite holds a similar view.
B) He points out that Mu'tazilite scholars like Ja'far ibn Harb and al-Ka'bi claim the Quran is in the Preserved Tablet and the revealed expressions indicate it.
C) He says the Quran is an uncreated object that descended physically.
D) He accepts the reproach and modifies his view.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi and Mu'tazilite views on Quranic revelation share a common core—the Quran consists of expressions pointing to divine speech—but differ on whether those expressions convey the Preserved Tablet's content or an attribute of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that even those who disagreed with the Maturidis held a similar core belief about divine speech. Are there areas in your own faith where you focus more on differences than on common ground with others? How might that change your interactions?
?
Al-Nasafi refutes an accusation by exposing the opponent's own scholars. When you feel unfairly criticized, do you seek to defend yourself by pointing out inconsistencies in the accuser? Or do you respond with humility? What would be more pleasing to Allah?

Accusing the Mu'tazila of Inconsistency: The Third Doubt
The author begins his refutation by accusing the Mu'tazila of inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi scholar refutes the Mu'tazila's third doubt by pointing out their own inconsistency.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own arguments refute their doubt about pre-eternal command.

The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot command or prohibit in pre-eternity because no one existed to receive the command. The author begins his refutation by turning this argument on its head: he accuses the Mu'tazila of using a method that would also undermine their own position. This lesson examines the opening stages of this refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text ends abruptly — the argument is incomplete.
2
Identify the accusation
The author accuses the Mu'tazila of inconsistency. Find the phrase where he says they are following the same path they criticise in others.
The key term is 'مناقضة' (contradiction).
3
Reflect on the method
Ask yourself: How does pointing out an opponent's inconsistency strengthen your own argument? This is a common dialectical strategy in kalam.
The passage hints that the Mu'tazila themselves rely on analogical reasoning from the witnessed realm, which they reject when used by others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حل الشبهة الثالثة. وهي القول باستحالة الأمر والنهي في الأزل مع انعدام المكلفين استدلالا بالشاهد على ما قرروا. فهو أن يقال لهم: قد علمتم معاشر المعتزلة أن التشنيع على الخصوم بشيء يرى المشنع عين ذلك الشيء حكمة وصوابا خارجا عن عادات العقلاء، وهو يعد أفحش مناقضة وأشد انقطاع، وأنتم في هذا السؤال وإثارة هذه الشبهة سالكون هذا المسلك لازمون هذه الطريقة، وبيان هذا أن عندكم كان المنزل على
As for solving the third doubt, which is the claim of the impossibility of command and prohibition in pre-eternity due to the non-existence of obligated beings, using evidence from the witnessed [realm] as they established. The response is to say to them: You, group of Mu'tazila, know that casting blame on opponents for something that the blamer himself exhibits is outside the ways of rational people, and it is considered the most severe contradiction and most extreme weakness. And you, in this question and raising this doubt, are following this path and adhering to this method. The explanation of this is that according to you, what was sent down...
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage addresses the third doubt: how can God command and prohibit in pre-eternity when there are no people to obey? The author begins by accusing the Mu'tazila of hypocrisy: they criticize others for things they themselves do. The text is cut off, but the argument likely continues to show that the Mu'tazila themselves hold that God's knowledge and power are eternal without objects, so command and prohibition can be eternal as well.

Key Terms

معتزلة
أمر

مناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commands and prohibitions cannot be eternal because they require a subject to exist at the same time.
Correction: The author argues that the Mu'tazila's own use of analogical reasoning from the witnessed realm undermines this claim, as they accept eternal attributes without objects in other cases.

Checkpoint

A) God cannot command and prohibit in pre-eternity because no one exists
B) God's speech is created
C) Commands and prohibitions are opposites
D) The example of the man and his slaves is invalid

Key Takeaway
The refutation begins by exposing the Mu'tazila's own inconsistency in using the unseen-to-witness analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refutation of Suspending Judgment on the Quran
Imam Nasafi refutes those who suspend judgment on the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why suspending judgment on the Quran's createdness is untenable according to Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
Suspending judgment is not neutral; evidence demands a clear position.

Imam Nasafi continues his defense of the uncreated Quran by addressing those who try to avoid the debate. While claiming to stick to the agreed-upon fact that the Quran is Allah's speech, they refuse to take a position on its createdness. The author argues that this suspension is invalid given the proofs already established.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi describes the position he refutes.
The text is incomplete, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the position being refuted
Find the description of Abdullah al-Thalji's view. What does he say about the Quran? What does he refuse to add?
He says only 'Quran is speech of Allah' and stops.
3
Understand why it is invalid
Nasafi says 'by stopping at this as well, the invalidity is known.' Reflect on why earlier proofs would make suspension impossible.
The proof is cut off, but the implication is that evidence already shows the Quran is uncreated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالوقوف على هذا أيضا عرف بطلان قول من يتوقف في القرآن فيقول: القرآن كلام الله ولا يزيد على هذا، فلا يقول: إنه مخلوق أو غير مخلوق وهذا القول منسوب إلى عبد الله الثلجي ويقول: أقول بالمتفق عليه، وهو أن القرآن كلام الله، وأتوقف عند الاختلاف فلا أقول مخلوق أو غير مخلوق، وذلك لأنا أقمنا
And by stopping at this as well, the invalidity of the statement of one who suspends judgment about the Quran is known. He says: The Quran is the speech of Allah, and does not add to this. Thus he does not say: It is created or uncreated. And this statement is attributed to Abdullah al-Thalji. And he says: I say what is agreed upon, which is that the Quran is the speech of Allah, and I suspend judgment at the point of disagreement, so I do not say created or uncreated. And that is because we have established
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage refutes those who suspend judgment on whether the Quran is created or uncreated. Imam Nasafi states that after establishing the previous evidences, the invalidity of such suspension is clear. He describes the position: the person says 'the Quran is the speech of Allah' and nothing more, neither affirming nor denying that it is created. He attributes this view to Abdullah al-Thalji, who argues that he only holds to what is agreed upon (that the Quran is Allah's speech) and suspends judgment on the disputed issue. The text breaks off abruptly with 'And that is because we have established' (wa dhalika li-anna aqamna), leaving the argument incomplete. However, the intended refutation is that the earlier proofs have already demonstrated that the Quran is uncreated, so suspension is untenable.

Key Terms
توقف (suspension/judgment)
عبد الله الثلجي (Abdullah al-Thalji)
متفق عليه (agreed upon)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think suspending judgment on the Quran's createdness is a safe, neutral position that avoids error.
Correction: Nasafi argues that when clear proofs exist, suspension itself becomes invalid. One must take a position based on evidence.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Abdullah al-Thalji regarding the Quran as reported by Imam Nasafi?
A) He says the Quran is created
B) He says the Quran is uncreated
C) He says the Quran is the speech of Allah and suspends judgment on its createdness
D) He says the Quran is an attribute of Allah

Key Takeaway
Suspending judgment on the Quran's createdness is invalid because established proofs already require a clear affirmation that it is uncreated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your faith have you settled for a 'safe middle ground' on a matter of truth, knowing deep down that the evidence is clear? What is that issue, and what will you do about it today?
?
When you encounter disagreement among Muslims about a theological or practical issue, do you retreat to 'only what is agreed upon' to avoid discomfort? In what specific area of your life is that intellectual laziness actually a failure to submit to the truth?
?
This lesson condemns suspending judgment after proof has been established. Is there a command of Allah or His Messenger that you have heard, understood, yet still postpone acting upon—telling yourself you are merely 'agreeing on the basics'? What step can you take within the next 24 hours to end that suspension?

Opponents' Challenge: Is Takwīn an Innovation?
Opponents argue the doctrine of takwīn is a novel opinion introduced later.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how opponents of the Maturidi doctrine of eternal attributes accused it of being a late innovation with no precedent among the Salaf.

Core Idea
The Maturidi doctrine of takwīn was dismissed as a foreign innovation by its detractors.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents the first of two objections raised by opponents against the Maturidi doctrine of eternal attributes (takwīn). The opponents claim that this doctrine is an innovation (ḥadtuth) introduced by later generations, originating not in the heartlands of Islamic scholarship but in the peripheral region of Samarkand. They even attribute it to an obscure sect called al-Zabarshā'iyya. Nasafi records these allegations before he proceeds to refute them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the opponents' rhetorical strategy. Notice the term 'tashnī'āt' (objections) and how Nasafi frames their argument.
The passage is incomplete; Nasafi will respond later.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
The opponents raise two points: (1) the doctrine has no precedent among the Salaf, (2) it originated from outside Iraq, specifically Samarkand, attributed to a sect called al-Zabarshā'iyya.
Only the first aspect is elaborated in this segment.
3
Consider the opponents' strategy
Ask yourself why opponents would emphasize the novelty and foreign origin of the doctrine. In Islamic theological debates, labeling a view as an innovation (bid'a) is a strong rhetorical move to discredit it.
Nasafi will later refute these claims by showing that the doctrine is rooted in the Quran and Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الخصوم يشنعون علينا في هذه المسألة من وجهين: أحدهما: أن هذا قول أحدثتم أنتم لم يكن به قائل من السلف حتى قال بعضهم: إن هذا القول لم يأت من العراق، وإنما جاء من الأعالي يعني بذلك من سمرقند. وزعم بعض الأشعرية أن هذا قول أحدثته طائفة من الناس يقال لهم الزابرشائية أصحاب رجل يعرف بأي عاصم الزابرشائي. نبعت بمرو الأربعمائة من الهجرة، وذكر أبو عبد الله محمد بن هيضم الساتي عواره لبداية العامة من مذاهب الكرامية بالحيل الضعيفة على ضعفاء أصحابه – بعد لما ظهر
Then the opponents raise objections against us in this issue from two aspects: One of them: that this is a statement you have innovated, for which there was no proponent among the Salaf, until some of them said: This statement did not come from Iraq, but rather came from the highlands, meaning from Samarkand. And some Ash'aris claimed that this statement was innovated by a group of people called al-Zabarshā'iyya, followers of a man known as Abu 'Asim al-Zabarshā'i. It emerged in Marw in the four hundredth year of the Hijra. And Abu 'Abdullah Muhammad ibn Haydam al-Sati mentioned its defects for the benefit of the common people from the doctrines of the Karramiyya with weak tricks upon his weak companions – after it appeared.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two objections that opponents raise against the Maturidi doctrine of eternal attributes (especially takwīn). The objection claims that this doctrine is an innovation (ḥadtuth) with no precedent among the Salaf. It is alleged to have originated not in Iraq (the center of Islamic scholarship) but from the highlands (al-aʿālī), i.e., Samarkand. Some Ash'aris further claim that the doctrine was created by a sect called al-Zabarshā'iyya, followers of Abu 'Asim al-Zabarshā'i, who appeared in Marw around the year 400 AH. The passage then mentions Ibn Haydam al-Sati's criticism of the Karramiyya, though the sentence is incomplete. Nasafi is reporting these objections here, not yet responding to them.

Key Terms
تشنيعات
زابرشائية
أشعرية
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Nasafi accepts these objections as valid, but he is merely reporting them.
Correction: Nasafi is presenting the opponents' view for refutation; his own position is that the doctrine of takwīn is sound and grounded in revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the opponents in this passage, where did the doctrine of eternal attributes supposedly originate?
A) Iraq
B) Samarkand
C) Marw
D) Kufa

Key Takeaway
Opponents attempted to discredit the Maturidi doctrine of takwīn by labeling it a baseless innovation with foreign origins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponents used the accusation of innovation to discredit a truth they found inconvenient. Are there truths about your own faith or practice that you avoid because they come from unfamiliar sources? Are you willing to accept a truth even if it challenges your comfort zone?
?
Reflect on your own study of aqeedah: are you quick to label ideas as 'bid'ah' without thoroughly understanding what the Salaf actually said? What steps can you take to ensure your judgments are based on evidence rather than hearsay or prejudice?

The First Objection: Eternal Takwin as Innovation
Nasafi reports the first objection that eternal takwin is an innovation attributed to later groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first historical objection to the Maturidi doctrine of eternal takwin and how Nasafi frames its accusation.

Core Idea
The first attack on eternal takwin claims it is a later innovation, not from the Salaf.

In this passage, Nasafi presents the first of two objections raised by opponents against the Maturidi position on takwin. The objection argues that the doctrine of eternal takwin is a historical innovation unknown to the early Muslims. Nasafi then provides a detailed account of the accusation, linking it to specific groups and regions, setting the stage for his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two parts
The objection has two aspects: (1) the doctrine is an innovation, and (2) it is attributed to a specific sect. Identify these in the text.
2
Notice the historical attributions
The objection traces the doctrine to the Zabirshaniyya, Ibn Kullab, and the region of Samarqand. Observe how Nasafi reports these claims without endorsing them.
3
Observe Nasafi's subtle correction
Nasafi notes that Maturidis trace their doctrine to Abu Hanifa, not Ibn Kullab, subtly distancing themselves from the accusation.
This is key to understanding the sectarian context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الخصوم يشنعون علينا في هذه المسألة من وجهين: أحدهما: أن هذا قول أحدثتم أنتم لم يكن به قائل من السلف حتى قال بعضهم: إن هذا القول لم يأت من العراق، وإنما جاء من الأعالي يعني بذلك من سمرقند. وزعم بعض الأشعرية أن هذا قول أحدثته طائفة من الناس يقال لهم الزابرشائية أصحاب رجل يعرف بأي عاصم الزابرشائي. نبعت بمرو الأربعمائة من الهجرة، وذكر أبو عبد الله محمد بن هيضم الساتي عواره لبداية العامة من مذاهب الكرامية بالحيل الضعيفة على ضعفاء أصحابه – بعد لما ظهر هذا القول ونسبه إلى المنتسبين إلى ابن كلاب من أهل مرو وسمر مرو وسمرقند عنى بذلك عبد الله بن سعيد القطان، وإنما نسبهم إليه لأن أهل السنة و الجماعة كانوا ينتسبون إليه، وإن كان أصحابنا أخذوا المذهب عن أبي حنيفة، قال : وأظن هؤلاء قد انقرضوا.
Then the opponents attack us in this matter in two ways: One of them: that this is a saying which you have innovated, for which there was no proponent among the Salaf, until some of them said: This saying did not come from Iraq, but rather came from the highlands, meaning thereby from Samarqand. And some Ash'aris claimed that this is a saying innovated by a group of people called al-Zabirshaniyya, followers of a man known as Abu 'Asim al-Zabirshani. It originated in Marw in the fourth century of the Hijra, and Abu 'Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Haytham al-Sati mentioned its defects in 'Bidayat al-'Amma' from the doctrines of the Karamiyya with weak tricks against the weak of his companions – after this saying appeared and he attributed it to those affiliated with Ibn Kullab from the people of Marw and Samarqand, meaning thereby 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan. He attributed them to him because Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a used to attribute themselves to him, although our companions took the school from Abu Hanifa. He said: I think these people have become extinct.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (tashni') against the Maturidi doctrine of eternal takwin. The author reports two objections, and here he details the first. The objection is twofold: the doctrine is historically an innovation without support from the Salaf, and it is attributed to a specific group, the Zabirshaniyya. Nasafi then provides more background: some said the doctrine originated in Samarqand, not Iraq. The Ash'ari accuser claims a sect called al-Zabirshaniyya started it. Nasafi narrates that Abu 'Abd Allah ibn Haytham al-Sati attacked this view in his book 'Bidayat al-'Amma', linking it to the Karamiyya and Ibn Kullab. However, Nasafi notes that the Maturidis trace their doctrine back to Abu Hanifa, not Ibn Kullab. The objection concludes by suggesting that the proponents of this view have died out. This passage shows the historical and sectarian nature of the criticism.

Key Terms
تشنيع
الخصوم
أحدثتم
السلف
الأشعرية
الزابرشائية
ابن كلاب
أبو حنيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Maturidi doctrine of eternal takwin is an innovation introduced by later groups like the Ash'aris or Ibn Kullab.
Correction: Nasafi insists that the Maturidis took the doctrine from Abu Hanifa, not from later figures, and that the accusers themselves may have distorted the history.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, from where did the doctrine of eternal takwin supposedly originate?
A) From Iraq
B) From Samarqand (the highlands)
C) From Mecca
D) From Damascus

Key Takeaway
The first objection against eternal takwin is that it is a historical innovation, but Nasafi traces it back to Abu Hanifa, establishing its orthodox roots.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted or rejected a religious belief simply because someone labelled it 'new' or 'old'? How can you ensure that your convictions are based on truth rather than on the age or popularity of an idea?
?
The opponents in this passage used geography and group affiliation to discredit a doctrine. Have you ever judged someone's faith based on their ethnic background, school of thought, or teacher? What would it take for you to evaluate a belief purely on its evidence?
?
Nasafi notes that the accused group has become extinct. Do you rely on the survival of a doctrine across generations as a sign of its validity? If so, how do you distinguish between a truth that was always held by the majority and a truth that was only known by a small minority?

Critique of al-Ash'ari's Grammatical Argument
Criticism and dilemma regarding al-Ash'ari's use of Sibawayh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's criticism of al-Ash'ari's use of Sibawayh and the dilemma that exposes his inconsistency.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's own grammarian contradicts his theology.

This passage opens with a sharp critique of al-Ash'ari's engagement with grammar. The author argues that al-Ash'ari lacked proper knowledge of the discipline, then presents a dilemma: either al-Ash'ari accepts Sibawayh's grammar entirely, which undermines his denial of human agency, or he rejects it, invalidating his proof. This introductory section sets the stage for a deeper grammatical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the tone of critique.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two horns: either al-Ash'ari accepts all of Sibawayh's statements or he does not. See how each horn leads to a problem for him.
3
Reflect on the connection to human agency
Understand how Sibawayh's example 'Zaid struck' affirms human acts, directly contradicting al-Ash'ari's denial.
This shows the dilemma is not just logical but theological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما استدل به من قول سيبويه في حد الفعل أنه أمثلة أخذت من لفظة أحداث الأسماء إلى آخر الفصل . فنقول : إن هذا الرجل تعاطى صناعة نظر إليها من وراء الجبال لا من وراء الجدار ، ولو عرف موضوعات أهل النحو وما هو فعل عندهم وما ليس بفعل وما هو مفعول لهم وما ليس بمفعول. لما اشتغل بمثل هذا الكلام الدال على خلوه من هذه الصناعة. وقبل أن نشرع في شرح هذا فنقول: أكل ما أطلقه مأخوذ به؟ فإن قال : لا أبطل احتجاجه به والتعلق سيبويه صحيح بقوله. وإن قال: نعم، قيل : أن سيبويه أثبت للعباد فعلا، فإنه قال في قوله: ضرب زيد ارتفع بفعله، فلم زعم صاحبكم الأشعري أن لا فعل في الحقيقة لغير الله تعالى وخالف سيبويه ثم قال : حد الفعل أمثلة أخذت من لفظ أحداث الأسماء، وأراد بالأحداث المصادر، وسماها أحداثا لأنها تحدث في رأي العين من الفاعلين، وأضاف الأحداث إلى الأسماء وأراد به الذوات التي يحصل منها الأفعال الحقيقية التي هي عندهم المصادر وسماها الأسماء، لأن الاسم عبارة عن المسمى، وهذا مما رده عليه أبو العباس المبرد وقال : هذا خطأ لأن المصادر تحدث من المسميات لا من الأسماء، والصحيح أن يقال : أحداث أصحاب الأسماء كذا ذكر في كتابه المسمى بالانتقاد على سيبويه ذكر فيه أشياء استدركها عليه. وأهل
And what he adduced from the saying of Sibawayh on the definition of the verb: that it is patterns taken from the expression 'events of names' up to the end of the section. So we say: This man engaged in a craft he looked at from beyond the mountains, not from beyond the wall. Had he known the subject matters of the grammarians, what is a verb for them and what is not a verb, what is an object for them and what is not an object, he would not have busied himself with such talk indicating his void of this craft. Before we begin explaining this, we say: Is everything he has uttered to be accepted? If he says: No, his argument by it is invalid, and his reliance on Sibawayh's correct statement fails. If he says: Yes, it is said: Sibawayh affirmed an act for servants, for he said in his statement: 'Zaid struck' is raised by his act, so why did your master al-Ash'ari claim that there is no act in reality for other than God, contradicting Sibawayh? Then he said: The definition of the verb is patterns taken from the expression 'events of names'. He meant by 'events' the verbal nouns, and he called them 'events' because they occur in the eye of the beholder from the agents. He added 'events' to 'names' and meant by them the entities from which the real acts (which for them are the verbal nouns) occur, and he called them 'names' because the name is an expression for the named. This is what Abu al-Abbas al-Mubarrad refuted him on, saying: This is wrong because the verbal nouns occur from the entities named, not from the names. The correct expression is 'events of the owners of names.' Thus it is mentioned in his book called 'al-Intiqad 'ala Sibawayh', in which he mentioned things he objected to him. And the people...
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical refutation of al-Ash'ari's attempt to use Sibawayh's definition of the verb to deny human agency. The author (al-Nasafi) begins by criticizing al-Ash'ari's engagement in grammar without proper knowledge, likening his view to one looking from afar. He then poses a dilemma: if al-Ash'ari does not accept every statement of Sibawayh, his proof is invalid; if he does accept, then Sibawayh himself affirms human acts, as seen in his example 'Zaid struck' which is raised by its agent's act. Al-Ash'ari contradicted Sibawayh by denying real acts for creatures. The author then explains Sibawayh's definition: the verb is patterns taken from 'events of names'. Sibawayh meant by 'events' the verbal nouns (masādir), which are called events because they occur from agents. He added 'events' to 'names', meaning the entities (dhāt) from which real acts proceed. However, Abu al-Abbas al-Mubarrad refuted this, pointing out that the verbal nouns occur from the entities named (musammayāt), not from the names themselves; the correct expression is 'events of the owners of names'. This refutation is recorded in al-Mubarrad's book 'al-Intiqad 'ala Sibawayh'. The passage ends with 'And the people...' likely continuing the discussion of grammarians, but the page cuts off.

Key Terms
الفعل
المصدر
الاسم
سيبويه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Sibawayh's definition of verb denies human agency.
Correction: Sibawayh's example 'Zaid struck' shows he affirmed human acts; al-Ash'ari misused his definition.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does the author pose to al-Ash'ari?
A) Either al-Ash'ari accepts all of Sibawayh's grammar or he rejects it.
B) Either al-Ash'ari affirms human acts or he denies them.
C) Either al-Ash'ari is a grammarian or a theologian.
D) Either al-Ash'ari follows Sibawayh or follows the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
The author uses a logical dilemma to show that al-Ash'ari's reliance on Sibawayh is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author accuses al-Ash'ari of speaking about grammar without understanding it. In what area of your own life do you speak with confidence but lack true knowledge? When will you humble yourself to learn before you speak?
?
Al-Ash'ari used Sibawayh to support his denial of human acts, but Sibawayh's own example shows Zaid striking. Have you ever twisted evidence to support a comfortable belief? Will you now look at that evidence honestly and let it change your mind?
?
The dilemma forces al-Ash'ari into inconsistency: either reject Sibawayh or accept human acts. Are there beliefs you hold that force you into similar contradictions? What would it cost you to resolve them – and will you pay that price?

Sibawayh's Definition and al-Mubarrad's Correction
Explanation and correction of Sibawayh's definition of the verb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sibawayh's definition of the verb and the grammatical correction offered by al-Mubarrad.

Core Idea
Sibawayh's 'events of names' refers to acts performed by agents, not linguistic abstractions.

After the dilemma, the author explains Sibawayh's actual definition of the verb as 'patterns taken from the expression 'events of names' '. He clarifies that by 'events' Sibawayh meant verbal nouns (masādir) that occur from agents, and by 'names' he meant the entities (dhāt) from which these acts proceed. However, al-Mubarrad objected that verbal nouns occur from the entities named, not from the names themselves, and should be phrased as 'events of the owners of names'. This lesson unpacks that grammatical debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of Sibawayh's definition
Understand what Sibawayh meant by 'events' and 'names'. Events = verbal nouns; names = entities.
2
Identify al-Mubarrad's correction
See how al-Mubarrad refines the wording to avoid a category mistake.
The correction is subtle but important for theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما استدل به من قول سيبويه في حد الفعل أنه أمثلة أخذت من لفظة أحداث الأسماء إلى آخر الفصل . فنقول : إن هذا الرجل تعاطى صناعة نظر إليها من وراء الجبال لا من وراء الجدار ، ولو عرف موضوعات أهل النحو وما هو فعل عندهم وما ليس بفعل وما هو مفعول لهم وما ليس بمفعول. لما اشتغل بمثل هذا الكلام الدال على خلوه من هذه الصناعة. وقبل أن نشرع في شرح هذا فنقول: أكل ما أطلقه مأخوذ به؟ فإن قال : لا أبطل احتجاجه به والتعلق سيبويه صحيح بقوله. وإن قال: نعم، قيل : أن سيبويه أثبت للعباد فعلا، فإنه قال في قوله: ضرب زيد ارتفع بفعله، فلم زعم صاحبكم الأشعري أن لا فعل في الحقيقة لغير الله تعالى وخالف سيبويه ثم قال : حد الفعل أمثلة أخذت من لفظ أحداث الأسماء، وأراد بالأحداث المصادر، وسماها أحداثا لأنها تحدث في رأي العين من الفاعلين، وأضاف الأحداث إلى الأسماء وأراد به الذوات التي يحصل منها الأفعال الحقيقية التي هي عندهم المصادر وسماها الأسماء، لأن الاسم عبارة عن المسمى، وهذا مما رده عليه أبو العباس المبرد وقال : هذا خطأ لأن المصادر تحدث من المسميات لا من الأسماء، والصحيح أن يقال : أحداث أصحاب الأسماء كذا ذكر في كتابه المسمى بالانتقاد على سيبويه ذكر فيه أشياء استدركها عليه. وأهل

According to al-Mubarrad, what is the correct phrasing for Sibawayh's definition?
A) 'Events of names'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about your own sins or good deeds, do you ever disguise them with nicer names? How does al-Mubarrad's insistence on precise language challenge you to call your actions what they truly are?
?
If actions belong to the doer, not to the label, what does that mean for the way you take responsibility for your deeds? Are there areas of your life where you hide behind vague terms instead of owning your choices?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God or yourself that you've never examined closely. Could it be built on a confusion between a name and the reality it represents? What would it take to trace that belief back to its source and see if it holds up?

Ash'ari's Disagreement Undermines the Opponent's Argument
Nasafi's concluding rhetorical question exposing inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi's rhetorical question exposes an inconsistency in the opponent's reliance on Sibawayh's authority.

Core Idea
If your own leader disagrees, you cannot cite him as authority.

This lesson examines a powerful rhetorical move by Nasafi. After establishing that al-Ash'ari—the opponent's own imam—rejects Sibawayh's position on ism and musamma, Nasafi challenges the opponent's consistency. This is a classic kalam tactic: turning the opponent's authorities against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the rhetorical question.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage is a qiyas al-ilzam: an argument that points out inconsistency. Notice the conditional and the rhetorical question.
3
Relate to broader debate
Understand that this question concludes a discussion about whether lafz is the same as musamma. Nasafi is using the opponent's own sources.
This only works if the opponent accepts Ash'ari as authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان هو يخالف سيبويه في هذا كله، كيف ألزمتم خصومكم الأخذ بقوله والنزول على حكمه ؟
And if he [al-Ash'ari] disagrees with Sibawayh in all of this, how can you compel your opponents to accept his [Sibawayh's] statement and submit to his judgment?
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This brief but pointed rhetorical question serves as a conclusion to the previous discussion. Nasafi argues that since al-Ash'ari himself—whom the opponents follow—disagrees with Sibawayh on the entire issue of ism and musamma, the opponents have no logical ground to force their adversaries to accept Sibawayh's authority. The argument is: you (the opponent) cite Sibawayh as an authority, but your own imam has rejected Sibawayh; therefore, you cannot consistently demand that others accept Sibawayh's view. This is a typical kalam strategy of turning the opponent's own sources against them.

Key Terms
سيبويه
أشعري
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Nasafi is defending Sibawayh's position on ism and musamma.
Correction: Nasafi is not taking a side on Sibawayh's authority; he is exposing that the opponent's reliance on Sibawayh is inconsistent because their own imam rejected it.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi consider the opponent's use of Sibawayh's authority invalid?
A) Because Sibawayh was not a grammarian.
B) Because al-Ash'ari explicitly rejected Sibawayh's view.
C) Because the opponent does not understand grammar.
D) Because Sibawayh changed his mind.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's question shows how a theological argument can be defeated by pointing out the opponent's internal inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used an authority to support your position while ignoring that same authority's disagreement on a related issue? How will you correct this inconsistency in your own thinking?
?
In your own faith practice, are there areas where you rely on a scholar for one issue but disregard their teachings on another? How does Nasafi's question challenge you to be more consistent?
?
When you engage in debates about religion, do you apply the same standard of evidence to your own argument that you demand from others? How can you ensure you are not guilty of the same inconsistency Nasafi exposes?

Contradiction of Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya on Attributes
Al-Nasafi exposes the inconsistency of Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya regarding the principle of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi exposes the logical inconsistency of both the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya regarding the application of the principle of derived names (al-mushtaqqat).

Core Idea
Consistency is the first casualty of sectarian prejudice—both Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya fail their own principles.

Al-Nasafi continues his critique of rival theological schools by highlighting a glaring contradiction. While the Mu'tazila inconsistently apply their own principle of naming based on the act of bringing about, the Ash'ariyya, who had previously condemned this inconsistency, fall into an even worse contradiction by abandoning the principle when it suits them. This passage reveals how sectarian loyalty can undermine rational consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the flow of argument from the Mu'tazila to the Ash'ariyya.
The passage uses a comparative structure to expose inconsistency.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's inconsistency
Al-Nasafi says the Mu'tazila followed their principle in some cases (mutakallim, kafir) but abandoned it in others (mutaharrik, sakin). Find where this is mentioned.
The principle is naming based on the act of bringing about, not on the attribute's subsistence.
3
Analyze the Ash'ariyya's contradiction
Notice that the Ash'ariyya initially helped fault the Mu'tazila for this inconsistency, but then themselves committed a worse one by adopting the Mu'tazilite view on Khaliq (creator). This is called a 'gross contradiction'.
This shows that even those who criticize inconsistency can fall into it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن المعتزلة جروا على هذا الأصل في بعض المواضع وهو مسألة الكلام حيث جعلوا المتكلم من أوجد الكلام لا من قام به الكلام، وكذا جعلوا الكافر من أوجد الكفر، وإن كانوا مناقضين في ذلك تاركين هذا الأصل في المتحرك والساكن وغير ذلك على ما بينا فأما الأشعرية فقد ساعدونا على تخطئه المعتزلة في تلك المسائل ونسبتهم إلى المناقضة والخروج عن المعقول ثم ها هنا أتوا بما يوجب هدم تلك الأصول وتصحيح مذاهب الخصوم، وهذا تناقض فاحش.
And the second: that the Mu'tazila followed this principle in some places—namely the issue of speech (al-kalam), where they made 'the speaker' (al-mutakallim) the one who brings about speech, not the one in whom speech subsists, and likewise they made 'the disbeliever' (al-kafir) the one who brings about disbelief, although they are contradictory in that, abandoning this principle in the case of 'the moving' (al-mutaharrik) and 'the stationary' (al-sakin) and other than that, as we have explained. As for the Ash'ariyya, they helped us in faulting the Mu'tazila in those matters and attributing to them contradiction and departure from reason, then here they came with what necessitates the demolition of those principles and the correction of the doctrines of the opponents, and this is a gross contradiction.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is a detailed critique of inconsistency. Al-Nasafi presents a second point (al-thānī): the Mu'tazila applied the correct principle in some cases—like 'mutakallim' and 'kāfir'—by naming based on the act of bringing about, but they abandoned it in cases like 'mutaḥarrik' and 'sākin', where they accepted that the name refers to the one in whom the attribute subsists. Thus they contradict themselves. The Ash'aris, however, agreed with the Maturidis in criticizing the Mu'tazila for this contradiction, yet they themselves committed an even worse contradiction: in the case of 'Khāliq', they abandoned the principle and adopted the Mu'tazilite view, thereby undermining the very basis of the argument and strengthening the position of their opponents. Al-Nasafi calls this a 'gross contradiction' (tanāquḍ fāḥish).

Key Terms
تناقض
المعتزلة
الأشعرية
الأصل
المتكلم
الكافر
المتحرك
الساكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think only the Mu'tazila are inconsistent, but Al-Nasafi shows the Ash'aris are equally inconsistent, if not worse.
Correction: The Ash'ariyya, despite criticizing the Mu'tazila, fall into a greater contradiction by abandoning their own principle when it comes to the name 'Khaliq'.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Nasafi accuse the Ash'ariyya of in this passage?
A) Consistently following the principle of derived names.
B) Contradicting themselves by faulting the Mu'tazila but then adopting their view on 'Khaliq'.
C) Rejecting all derived names as metaphorical.
D) Agreeing with the Mu'tazila on all points.

Key Takeaway
Both the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya are guilty of applying their principles selectively, but al-Nasafi reserves his harshest criticism for the Ash'ariyya for abandoning their own position after condemning the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself criticizing someone for an inconsistency, are you willing to examine your own positions for the same fault? Where in your life do you apply standards to others that you do not apply to yourself?
?
The Ash'aris condemned the Mu'tazila for a contradiction, then fell into the same trap. In what area of your faith or practice are you most vulnerable to hypocrisy—claiming one principle but acting on another? Commit to one specific change this week.
?
This lesson reveals that consistency is a rare virtue even among scholars. Which belief or action in your life today is most in tension with the truth you profess? Will you sincerely seek to resolve it, or will you rationalize it away? Make a concrete plan.

Refutation of the Ash'ari Objection on Accident and Indwelling
Nasafi refutes the Ash'ari objection that act and object can be one just as accident and indwelling are one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi school refutes the Ash'ari objection that act and object are one by showing that the analogy with accident and indwelling fails for divine attributes.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari's own stance on divine attributes disproves his objection.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi responds to an Ash'ari objection that tries to equate act and object with accident and indwelling. The Ash'ari argues that since an accident is indwelling in a locus without a separate indwelling, similarly act and object could be one. Nasafi's refutation points out that this analogy contradicts the Ash'ari's own position on God's attributes, where He does not indwell His creation. The passage also contains a strong accusation of plagiarism, highlighting the polemical context.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Ash'ari's objection carefully: he claims that since an accident (al-‘araḍ) is indwelling (ḥāll) in a locus by an indwelling (ḥulūl) identical to itself, one can say the state and indwelling are one. He then extends this to say that the act and the object acted upon can be one.
Notice the analogy: accident:indwelling = act:object.
2
Follow the refutation
Nasafi begins his answer by citing al-Ash'ari's own book on attributes. There, al-Ash'ari stated that God's knowledge does not indwell Him—it is impossible to apply physical concepts like settling to divine attributes. Thus, the analogy of indwelling cannot apply to God.
Nasafi uses the Ash'ari's own authority against him.
3
Note the plagiarism charge
Nasafi accuses the objector of plagiarizing from the book 'al-Muqni‘' by Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub, stripping it and presenting it as his own. This undermines the objector's credibility and shows the argument is not original.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the refutation is clearly against the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأورد الأشعري المشنع علينا فقال: أليس أن العرض حال في المحل وهو حال بحلول هو نفسه ولم يلزم القول بأن الحلول غير الحال، فلما جاز القول باتحاد الحال والحلول جاز القول باتحاد الفعل والمفعول؟ والجواب عنه: أن يقال له، إنك أنت الذي ذكرت في مسألة الصفات في كتابك أن علم الباري لا يحله وليس هو محلا لعلمه، ولا لسائر صفات ذاته وإن كانت موجودة بذاته، لأن الحلول في اللغة هو السكون يقال حل فلان وارتحل، والعلم غير ساكن في العالم بل يستحيل أن توصف الصفة بالسكون والحركة، ثم زعمت وكذلك الحركة غير حالة في المتحرك لما ذكرنا غير أنه يستعمل في وصف غيره ألفاظ المجاز، فأما في أوصاف الباري جل وعلا فيراعى التوقيف والحقيقة. ثم ذكرت الخلاف بين أصحاب الصفات أن عبد الله بن سعيد وأبا العباس القلانسي وغيرهما من متقدمي أصحاب الصفات كانوا يقولون: إن علم الباري قائم به، والأشعري اختار عبارة أخرى، وهي أن صفات الباري موجودة بذات الباري، وذكر هذا كله غيرك من الأشعريين في كتبهم منهم أبو منصور بن أيوب ذكر في كتابه المترجم بالمقنع وهو الذي سلخته سلخا بل انتحلت أكثره بعبارته ونظمه وترتيبه ونسبت التصنيف إلى نفسك ولبسته على الضعفة من أصحابك وزوجته باسمك، ذكر هذه الفصول في ذلك الكتاب باللفظ الذي ذكرته، وكذا غير كما من الأشعرية، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، هل يكون هذا الإلزام منك باتحاد الحال والحلول إلا غفلة راكدة وتحكما ظاهرا ووقاحة ظاهرة ووقاحة بينة دفعتك إليها الحيرة في نحلتك. ثم نقول: لو كنا أبينا عليك القول باتحاد الفاعل والفعل فأوردت هذا لكان
Then al-Ash'ari raised a criticism against us and said: 'Is it not the case that an accident (al-‘araḍ) is indwelling (ḥāll) in a locus, and it is a state (ḥāl) by an indwelling (ḥulūl) that is itself, and one is not obliged to say that the indwelling is other than the state? And since it is permissible to say that the state and the indwelling are one, it is permissible to say that the act and the object acted upon are one?' The answer to him is that it is said to him: You yourself mentioned in the issue of the attributes in your book that the knowledge of the Creator does not indwell Him, and He is not a locus for His knowledge, nor for the rest of the attributes of His essence, even though they exist by His essence, because indwelling (ḥulūl) in language means settling (sukūn); it is said, 'So-and-so settled and departed,' and knowledge is not settled in the knower, rather it is impossible to describe an attribute with settling and motion. Then you claimed likewise that motion does not indwell the moving thing for what we mentioned, except that metaphorical expressions are used in describing other than Him. As for the descriptions of the Creator, Exalted and Majestic, one observes the dictated (tawqīf) and the literal. Then you mentioned the disagreement among the proponents of attributes: that 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id, Abu al-‘Abbas al-Qalanisi, and others of the early proponents of attributes used to say that the knowledge of the Creator subsists in Him, while al-Ash'ari chose another expression, namely that the attributes of the Creator exist by the essence of the Creator. And all of this was mentioned by others among the Ash'aris in their books, among them Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub, who mentioned in his book entitled al-Muqni‘, which you skinned thoroughly—rather, you plagiarized most of it with its wording, composition, and arrangement, attributing the authorship to yourself, dressing it up for the weak among your companions, and naming it after yourself—he mentioned these sections in that book with the very wording you mentioned, and likewise others among the Ash'aris. And if the matter is so, is this imputation from you of the union of state and indwelling anything other than a stagnant negligence, a manifest arbitrariness, a patent insolence, and a clear impudence, to which the confusion in your sect drove you? Then we say: If we had forbidden you from saying that the agent and the act are one, you would have brought this objection, then it would have been...
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Ash'ari objects that since an accident (al-‘araḍ) is 'indwelling' (ḥāll) in its locus by an indwelling (ḥulūl) that is identical to itself, there is no need to distinguish between the state and the indwelling. Therefore, it should be acceptable to say act and object are one, just as state and indwelling are one. The Maturidi answer is a detailed refutation. First, the Maturidi points to al-Ash'ari's own position on divine attributes: in his book, al-Ash'ari stated that God's knowledge does not 'indwell' Him; rather, ‘'thing'' subsists by His essence. Al-Ash'ari distinguished between literal and metaphorical language: physical concepts like 'settling' (sukūn) cannot apply to God's attributes. So his own principles reject the analogy of 'indwelling' for God. The Maturidi then accuses the Ash'ari of inconsistency: other Ash'ari scholars (like Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub) have written works that the objector plagiarized, merely changing the name. This plagiarism shows the objector's argument is not original and is based on confusion. The Maturidi concludes that the objection stems from heedlessness and arrogance. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument implies that even if the Maturidi forbade union of agent and act, the objection would be invalid because of the Ash'ari's own doctrines.

Key Terms
العرض (al-‘araḍ)
الحال (al-ḥāl)
الحلول (al-ḥulūl)
الصفات (al-ṣifāt)
التوقيف (al-tawqīf)
المجاز (al-majāz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari objection is logically sound because the analogy between accident-indwelling and act-object seems consistent.
Correction: The Maturidi shows that the analogy breaks down for God because al-Ash'ari himself denies indwelling for divine attributes. Thus the objection contradicts the Ash'ari's own theology.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does al-Ash'ari say about God's knowledge in his own book?
A) It indwells Him
B) It does not indwell Him
C) It is created
D) It is separate from His essence

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari objection fails because applying the concept of indwelling to God contradicts the Ash'ari's own doctrine that God's attributes do not indwell Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to apply the same standard of consistency to your own beliefs that Nasafi demands from al-Ash'ari? When you argue with others, do you use principles that you would reject if applied to yourself? Where in your life might you be guilty of such inconsistency?
?
Nasafi accuses the objector of presenting borrowed arguments as his own. Have you ever taken credit for an insight or opinion that was not truly yours? How does that affect your sincerity before Allah, especially when it comes to defending what you believe?
?
The lesson insists that words about God must be literal and precise. When you speak of Allah’s attributes, do you guard your tongue from careless or metaphorical language, or do you treat His names as ordinary words? What would it take to bring more reverence and accuracy to your speech?
